# Learning Excel: Data Analysis

> Microsoft Excel is an important tool for data analysis. It helps companies accurately assess situations and make better business decisions. This course helps you unlock the power of your organization's data using the data analysis and visualization tools built into Excel. Author Curt Frye starts with the foundational concepts, including basic calculations such as mean, median, and standard deviati

> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618) | 3h 41m | Intermediate | 56K learners

### 1. Foundational Concepts of Data Analysis

#### Calculate mean and median values
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-mean-and-median-values-24049637?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-mean-and-median-values-24049637?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Whenever you start to examine a new dataset, the first thing you should do is discover some basic facts about that data.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-mean-and-median-values-24049637?u=76281980&t=7)** In this movie, I will show you how to make several of those measures.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-mean-and-median-values-24049637?u=76281980&t=11)** My sample file is 01_01_Means, and you can find it in the chapter one folder of the Exercise Files collection.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-mean-and-median-values-24049637?u=76281980&t=19)** The dataset that I have in this workbook shows the number of days in transit for a number of shipments coming from overseas.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-mean-and-median-values-24049637?u=76281980&t=29)** So you can see the first shipment arrived in 23 days, the next in 38, and the rest of the data shows the other days that were measured.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-mean-and-median-values-24049637?u=76281980&t=39)** If you want to find the average or mean of that data, that would be the sum of all those values divided by the number of values.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-mean-and-median-values-24049637?u=76281980&t=47)** So if I go to cell D2, which is next to the mean label, and remember mean is the same as average, in D2, I can type equal and then the function we'll use is average, and I will select the data in the range A2 through A12 right parenthesis and enter, and we get a mean or average of 29.45 days.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-mean-and-median-values-24049637?u=76281980&t=76)** So just under 29.5 days on average.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-mean-and-median-values-24049637?u=76281980&t=81)** You can also calculate the median.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-mean-and-median-values-24049637?u=76281980&t=84)** The median is the middle value in a set of data that has been sorted into either ascending or descending order.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-mean-and-median-values-24049637?u=76281980&t=92)** For example, if I were to click cell A2, and then on the home tab of the ribbon, go to sort and filter and then click in this case sort smallest to largest, although I could do either largest to smallest or smallest to largest, then I get the values of 23, 27 all the way down to 38 I have, I'll just count here, 11 rows of data.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-mean-and-median-values-24049637?u=76281980&t=121)** So if I go down to the sixth, that's one, two, three, four, five, six.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-mean-and-median-values-24049637?u=76281980&t=126)** The median value is 28.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-mean-and-median-values-24049637?u=76281980&t=129)** So if I go to cell D4 and type equal, median and select A2 through A12, right parenthesis and enter, and I do in fact get 28.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-mean-and-median-values-24049637?u=76281980&t=145)** One way to examine your data is to compare the mean to the median, and in this case, they're very close.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-mean-and-median-values-24049637?u=76281980&t=152)** So that means that your data tends to be fairly well spread out.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-mean-and-median-values-24049637?u=76281980&t=155)** To give you an example of how the mean and median could change, let's say that we had a shipment that was just horrible.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-mean-and-median-values-24049637?u=76281980&t=162)** It took 75 days to arrive.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-mean-and-median-values-24049637?u=76281980&t=164)** So I'll go to cell A12 and change 38 to 75 and press enter.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-mean-and-median-values-24049637?u=76281980&t=170)** And you can see that the mean or average increased, but the median did not, and that's because the value of 28 is still in the middle of the sorted list.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-mean-and-median-values-24049637?u=76281980&t=180)** So I will press Ctrl Z to undo my last change, and we're back to our original dataset.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-mean-and-median-values-24049637?u=76281980&t=186)** One last thing about the median is that if you have an odd number of values, then the value in the middle of the sorted list is the median.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-mean-and-median-values-24049637?u=76281980&t=195)** If you have an even number of values, then Excel takes the average of the two that are in the middle.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-mean-and-median-values-24049637?u=76281980&t=202)** So in A2 through A12, we have 11 values.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-mean-and-median-values-24049637?u=76281980&t=205)** If I go to cell A13, and let's say I type in 39, then I can update the median formula so that it includes the new cell, A13, enter, and you see we get the median of 28.5, which is the average of the two values that are now in the middle of the list.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-mean-and-median-values-24049637?u=76281980&t=229)** I'll click to release the selection and press control + Z to undo the data entry and also the formula change.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-mean-and-median-values-24049637?u=76281980&t=237)** The final type of measure that I'll describe here is the mode, and that is the most frequently occurring value or values.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-mean-and-median-values-24049637?u=76281980&t=245)** If I go to cell D6, which is next to Mode single, I'll type equal, and then I'll type mode, and you see that underneath I have Mode.Mult, which is for multiple values or single, S-N-G-L.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-mean-and-median-values-24049637?u=76281980&t=263)** I'll click single for now and press tab, and I will select the same data range as before, A2 through A12, right parenthesis and enter, and we get 28.
>
> **[4:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-mean-and-median-values-24049637?u=76281980&t=275)** And if I go over to the dataset, I can see that 28 occurs three times, 27 twice, and 29 twice, and everyone else occurs a single time.
>
> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-mean-and-median-values-24049637?u=76281980&t=287)** So 28 is in fact the mode.
>
> **[4:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-mean-and-median-values-24049637?u=76281980&t=290)** If I were to change the value at the bottom from 38 to 27 and press enter, then you can see that the mode has changed to 27.
>
> **[5:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-mean-and-median-values-24049637?u=76281980&t=302)** And even though 27 and 28 occur the same number of times, 27 occurs first in the dataset.
>
> **[5:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-mean-and-median-values-24049637?u=76281980&t=309)** So that means that Excel will select it instead of going with the larger value.
>
> **[5:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-mean-and-median-values-24049637?u=76281980&t=317)** That's the rule it uses, the first value.
>
> **[5:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-mean-and-median-values-24049637?u=76281980&t=320)** If you do want to find multiple values that occur most frequently.
>
> **[5:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-mean-and-median-values-24049637?u=76281980&t=325)** In other words they're tied within a dataset, you can use Mode.Mult.
>
> **[5:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-mean-and-median-values-24049637?u=76281980&t=330)** So I'll go to cell D8, type in equal sign, mode M-U-L-T, and note that I still have my change from before.
>
> **[5:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-mean-and-median-values-24049637?u=76281980&t=339)** So I have 27 instead of 38.
>
> **[5:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-mean-and-median-values-24049637?u=76281980&t=342)** Select cells, A2 through A12, right parenthesis and enter, and we get 27 and 28.
>
> **[5:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-mean-and-median-values-24049637?u=76281980&t=350)** In the past, you would've had to identify the number of modes that you wanted to display using Mode.mult.
>
> **[5:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-mean-and-median-values-24049637?u=76281980&t=358)** However, in recent versions of Excel, there is the spill behavior, and that is what occurred here.
>
> **[6:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-mean-and-median-values-24049637?u=76281980&t=365)** Even though I typed the formula into cell D8, when I pressed enter because there were multiple values available, then it applied the same formula and spilled the result from cell D8 to D9.
>
> **[6:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-mean-and-median-values-24049637?u=76281980&t=382)** If I were to change the value in A12 from 27 back to 38, then you can see that the mode is still 28, and you can see that there is only one value that occurs most frequently in the data list.
>
> **[6:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-mean-and-median-values-24049637?u=76281980&t=400)** So as you can see, there are a lot of ways to get information about your data, whether the average, the middle value or median, or the most frequently occurring value or values.
>
> **[6:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-mean-and-median-values-24049637?u=76281980&t=409)** You can learn a lot in just a few minutes.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** data (12), next (3), [[Microsoft Excel|Excel]] (3), [[Representational State Transfer (REST)|Rest]] (1)

> **[4:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-data-using-variance-and-standard-deviation-24043677?u=76281980&t=278)** We are assuming that we don't have all of our data, so we're dividing by a slightly smaller number than we would otherwise so we have a more conservative estimate.
>
> **[4:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-data-using-variance-and-standard-deviation-24043677?u=76281980&t=289)** So I'll type a left parentheses and we are taking the variance of the sample in cells A4 to A13, right parenthesis and enter.
>
> **[5:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-data-using-variance-and-standard-deviation-24043677?u=76281980&t=300)** And as you can see, we get 7,106,604.
>
> **[5:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-data-using-variance-and-standard-deviation-24043677?u=76281980&t=306)** And if I go down to cell F18 and type equal stdev.s again for the sample and A4 through A13, right parenthesis and tab, then we get a standard deviation of 2,666.
>
> **[5:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-data-using-variance-and-standard-deviation-24043677?u=76281980&t=324)** I wanted to show you the mechanics of how variants of standard deviation or calculated so you have an idea of what's going on when Excel makes those calculations for you.
>
> **[5:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-data-using-variance-and-standard-deviation-24043677?u=76281980&t=336)** With variance of standard deviation in mind, you'll get a lot out of the rest of the course.

#### Introduce the central limit theorem
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-the-central-limit-theorem-24048666?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-the-central-limit-theorem-24048666?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] One of the dangers of business data analysis is making decisions too soon.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-the-central-limit-theorem-24048666?u=76281980&t=5)** The reason is that short-term results can be deceiving, but you should start to see patterns as you gather more data.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-the-central-limit-theorem-24048666?u=76281980&t=12)** One reliable principle of data analysis is the central limit theorem, which says that as the number of measurements increases, the more likely it is that your data will be distributed as you expect.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-the-central-limit-theorem-24048666?u=76281980&t=24)** As an example, let's say that your data is normally distributed and a normal distribution has an average and also a standard deviation.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-the-central-limit-theorem-24048666?u=76281980&t=34)** In this case, we're looking at a so-called normal curve with a mu or average value of 100 and a standard deviation of 20.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-the-central-limit-theorem-24048666?u=76281980&t=44)** And you can see a curve of values on this graph.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-the-central-limit-theorem-24048666?u=76281980&t=49)** And on the left, in the vertical axis, there is the probability of a specific value occurring.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-the-central-limit-theorem-24048666?u=76281980&t=54)** So you can see that the chance of getting exactly 100 is 2%.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-the-central-limit-theorem-24048666?u=76281980&t=59)** That's pretty low, but there are a lot of values clustered around it And that is where the power of the normal curve and normal distribution comes into play.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-the-central-limit-theorem-24048666?u=76281980&t=69)** If your data is normally distributed, and a lot of it is, then you should expect to see about 68% of your values in your dataset within one standard deviation, plus or minus of the mean.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-the-central-limit-theorem-24048666?u=76281980&t=81)** In this case, that would mean that 68% of your values would be between 80 and 120.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-the-central-limit-theorem-24048666?u=76281980&t=87)** So again, that's the average or mean of 100 minus 20 for 80 and plus 20 for 120.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-the-central-limit-theorem-24048666?u=76281980&t=96)** You can also expect to see about 95% of your values within two standard deviations, plus or minus between 60 and 140, and approximately 99.7% of values within three standard deviations, plus or minus.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-the-central-limit-theorem-24048666?u=76281980&t=111)** And of course, the probabilities of seeing other values get smaller as you go further away from the average.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-the-central-limit-theorem-24048666?u=76281980&t=117)** It doesn't mean they never occur, but it does mean that they are very rare.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-the-central-limit-theorem-24048666?u=76281980&t=123)** To show you how this data works in practice, I will switch to an Excel workbook, which you can find in the exercise files collection to use a macro to generate random values and show you what it looks like in practice.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-the-central-limit-theorem-24048666?u=76281980&t=135)** The workbook I'm using is 01_04_Central Limit Theorem.

#### Analyze a population using data samples
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-population-using-data-samples-24044689?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-population-using-data-samples-24044689?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When you gather data about your business, you will often analyze samples instead of an entire data set, and that's because gathering data can be very expensive, both in terms of money and time.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-population-using-data-samples-24044689?u=76281980&t=13)** That said, you should gather as large a sample as you can, interview as many customers, review as many products to make sure that you have all the data that you need.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-population-using-data-samples-24044689?u=76281980&t=22)** More is better.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-population-using-data-samples-24044689?u=76281980&t=24)** From there you can estimate the data population's standard deviation based on that sample, and then you determine your desired confidence level.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-population-using-data-samples-24044689?u=76281980&t=33)** Do you want to be 90% confident in your result?
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-population-using-data-samples-24044689?u=76281980&t=36)** Do you want to be 95% confident, 98, 99?
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-population-using-data-samples-24044689?u=76281980&t=40)** It all depends on how precise you want to be.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-population-using-data-samples-24044689?u=76281980&t=43)** From there, you can calculate your margin of error and that will tell you at your given confidence level how much above or below your values will be.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-population-using-data-samples-24044689?u=76281980&t=53)** So what is margin of error?
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-population-using-data-samples-24044689?u=76281980&t=56)** Well, let's say that each bottle contains 12 ounces of olive oil with a margin of error of plus or minus 0.3 ounces at a 95% level of confidence.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-population-using-data-samples-24044689?u=76281980&t=68)** So you want to be 95% confident that the true margin of error is plus or minus 0.3 ounces based on the mean of 12.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-population-using-data-samples-24044689?u=76281980&t=79)** You rarely see the confidence level stated explicitly in political polls or in business analysis, but it is always there and it is useful information.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-population-using-data-samples-24044689?u=76281980&t=89)** You calculate the margin of error first by calculating your standard error, and that is the standard deviation of the dataset in this case, the sample that you've taken and dividing that by the square root of the number of measurements.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-population-using-data-samples-24044689?u=76281980&t=105)** Margin of error is then standard error times your Z-score.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-population-using-data-samples-24044689?u=76281980&t=109)** And the Z-score is the number of standard deviations that your confidence level is from the mean.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-population-using-data-samples-24044689?u=76281980&t=115)** A Z-score of one includes about 68% of values, but being 68% certain about something is not reliable, I recommend going for higher values and I have those listed in the chart on this slide.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-population-using-data-samples-24044689?u=76281980&t=130)** Here we have some of the commonly used Z-scores and you can see the confidence level and the Z-score that is associated with them.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-population-using-data-samples-24044689?u=76281980&t=138)** Most analysis in business operates at the 90% or 95% confidence levels.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-population-using-data-samples-24044689?u=76281980&t=144)** So Z-score of 1.645 and 1.96 respectively.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-population-using-data-samples-24044689?u=76281980&t=151)** Getting enough samples to reach the 98% and 99% confidence levels can be expensive.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-population-using-data-samples-24044689?u=76281980&t=158)** If you can do it great, but 90 and 95% are much more common.

#### Identify and minimize sources of error
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/identify-and-minimize-sources-of-error-24049636?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/identify-and-minimize-sources-of-error-24049636?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Statistical analysis might seem like an exact science, but if you've ever tried to apply statistics to real life, you know that in fact, it is not.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/identify-and-minimize-sources-of-error-24049636?u=76281980&t=9)** In this movie, I'd like to review a number of potential sources of error that might creep into your own analysis, because once you know what they are, you can do your best to avoid them.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/identify-and-minimize-sources-of-error-24049636?u=76281980&t=19)** Not using random samples can be a huge source of error.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/identify-and-minimize-sources-of-error-24049636?u=76281980&t=23)** One famous example comes from the 1936 US presidential election where a telephone poll of "Literary Digest" subscribers projected that Alfred Landon would beat Franklin Delano Roosevelt by a wide margin.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/identify-and-minimize-sources-of-error-24049636?u=76281980&t=36)** In fact, Roosevelt won nearly 2/3 of the popular vote.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/identify-and-minimize-sources-of-error-24049636?u=76281980&t=40)** The error came from two sources.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/identify-and-minimize-sources-of-error-24049636?u=76281980&t=42)** "Literary Digest" was a conservative publication, which biased the results, and the poll was conducted by telephone.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/identify-and-minimize-sources-of-error-24049636?u=76281980&t=49)** In 1936, only the financially well-off had telephones in their homes, so that biased the results as well.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/identify-and-minimize-sources-of-error-24049636?u=76281980&t=57)** You can also run into investigator bias.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/identify-and-minimize-sources-of-error-24049636?u=76281980&t=60)** It's easy to anticipate what your data will tell you.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/identify-and-minimize-sources-of-error-24049636?u=76281980&t=63)** That's normal.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/identify-and-minimize-sources-of-error-24049636?u=76281980&t=64)** But you shouldn't let those expectations affect your judgment.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/identify-and-minimize-sources-of-error-24049636?u=76281980&t=67)** Many interesting discoveries come from the moment when you look at your data and think, that's strange, because the results don't fit your preconceived notions.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/identify-and-minimize-sources-of-error-24049636?u=76281980&t=78)** You can also run into trouble working with old data.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/identify-and-minimize-sources-of-error-24049636?u=76281980&t=81)** The world changes, and just because your customers might have been ready for a product two years ago doesn't mean they are now.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/identify-and-minimize-sources-of-error-24049636?u=76281980&t=89)** And finally, you can run into trouble basing your policy on a survey or experiment with a small sample.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/identify-and-minimize-sources-of-error-24049636?u=76281980&t=96)** The more data you can get, the better, and the greater variety of people that you ask, again, selected randomly, the better your analysis will be.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/identify-and-minimize-sources-of-error-24049636?u=76281980&t=107)** Hopefully, pointing out these sources there will help you in your own analysis, and you can use random data, eliminate your own personal bias, work with the newest data that you have, and have an adequate sample size.

>

>
> **[4:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-summarize-and-analyze-business-data-24048673?u=76281980&t=274)** So I'll type an equal sign and st dev S, again working with samples, which is a more conservative estimate.
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-summarize-and-analyze-business-data-24048673?u=76281980&t=283)** And then B 4 through B 33 and enter.
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-summarize-and-analyze-business-data-24048673?u=76281980&t=288)** And we get 499.27, which is the square root of the variance that we had earlier.
>
> **[4:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-summarize-and-analyze-business-data-24048673?u=76281980&t=296)** And looking at our values, we're looking at the average of 1319.47, and with a standard deviation of about 500, then one standard deviation above would be about 1,800, below would be about 800.
>
> **[5:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-summarize-and-analyze-business-data-24048673?u=76281980&t=312)** And given the range of values that we have with our maximum of almost 2,600 and our minimum of 180, it looks like our standard deviation represents the data in our collection pretty well.

#### Identify relationships using XY scatter charts
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/identify-relationships-using-xy-scatter-charts-24047700?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/identify-relationships-using-xy-scatter-charts-24047700?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] One common goal of performing business data analysis is to discover relationships between values.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/identify-relationships-using-xy-scatter-charts-24047700?u=76281980&t=6)** For example, you might own a store and want to determine whether individuals who come to the store from farther away are more likely to spend money once we get there.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/identify-relationships-using-xy-scatter-charts-24047700?u=76281980&t=15)** If you've collected that data, you could summarize it visually using what is known as an XY scatter chart.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/identify-relationships-using-xy-scatter-charts-24047700?u=76281980&t=22)** In this movie, I will show you how to create one.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/identify-relationships-using-xy-scatter-charts-24047700?u=76281980&t=25)** In the exercise files collection, you will find an Excel workbook called "O202 XY scatter," and that's an Excel workbook that contains the data that I will use in this movie.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/identify-relationships-using-xy-scatter-charts-24047700?u=76281980&t=36)** As you can see, I have two columns of data.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/identify-relationships-using-xy-scatter-charts-24047700?u=76281980&t=38)** In column A, I have the distance A customer traveled, and in column B, I have the amount that customer spent.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/identify-relationships-using-xy-scatter-charts-24047700?u=76281980&t=46)** So you can see that in row two, a customer that traveled one mile spent $35 and there was another within one mile who spent $13.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/identify-relationships-using-xy-scatter-charts-24047700?u=76281980&t=57)** And if we moved down the data set, which is sorted by distance, then we can see that it does appear that the farther away someone started from the store, that is the more they traveled to get there, the more they're likely to spend.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/identify-relationships-using-xy-scatter-charts-24047700?u=76281980&t=69)** But let's see what it looks like in an XY scatter chart.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/identify-relationships-using-xy-scatter-charts-24047700?u=76281980&t=72)** To create your chart, click any cell in your data set, then go to the insert tab, and in the charts group, click the insert scatter XY or bubble chart button and then at the top in the scatter group, just click the first chart subtype.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/identify-relationships-using-xy-scatter-charts-24047700?u=76281980&t=94)** When you do, Excel creates an XY scatter chart, and you can see that there is a fairly strong relationship between distance traveled and amount spent.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/identify-relationships-using-xy-scatter-charts-24047700?u=76281980&t=104)** You can see distance on the horizontal or X axis and amount spent on the Y or vertical axis.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/identify-relationships-using-xy-scatter-charts-24047700?u=76281980&t=113)** There's a definite upward trend, although you can also see that one individual who traveled 45 miles only spent thirty five dollars, but in general, the farther away someone started from your store, the more they spend when they get there.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/identify-relationships-using-xy-scatter-charts-24047700?u=76281980&t=130)** So it's important to remember that an XY scatter chart only works if you have two sets of numerical data that are matched as pairs.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/identify-relationships-using-xy-scatter-charts-24047700?u=76281980&t=139)** If you have, say, months of the year, then an XY scatter chart is probably not your best bet because months are categorical data as opposed to numerical.

> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/add-trendlines-to-charts-24048668?u=76281980&t=179)** We have collected data on individuals who traveled up to 150 miles to get to your store, and the question is, if this trend holds, if someone travels, say 300 miles, would they actually spend $400 typically?

> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/add-trendlines-to-charts-24048668?u=76281980&t=195)** That doesn't seem likely, and that's the sort of judgment you have to make about your business.

> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/add-trendlines-to-charts-24048668?u=76281980&t=206)** If someone travels 160 miles, even though it's more than 152, you can probably make reasonable assumptions about how much they're going to spend.

#### Forecast future results
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/forecast-future-results-24043682?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/forecast-future-results-24043682?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Presenter] When you own or operate a business, you're always wondering what happens next.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/forecast-future-results-24043682?u=76281980&t=5)** In this movie, I will show you a couple of techniques you can use to answer those questions at least as far as current trends allow.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/forecast-future-results-24043682?u=76281980&t=13)** My sample file is 02_05_Forecast and you can find it in the chapter two folder of the exercise files collection.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/forecast-future-results-24043682?u=76281980&t=20)** I'm on the trend worksheet in this workbook and you can see that I have quarterly sales data for the years of 2021 and 2022.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/forecast-future-results-24043682?u=76281980&t=31)** What I'd like to do is to get a forecast based on those values for the quarters in 2023, and you can do that using the fill-handle.
>

> **[5:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-running-averages-24045729?u=76281980&t=330)** But now if I double click the fill handle next to E4, you can see that my last three months running average changes.
>
> **[5:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-running-averages-24045729?u=76281980&t=340)** And that's because it keeps looking at the values from the previous three months.
>
> **[5:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-running-averages-24045729?u=76281980&t=347)** So in cell E5, we're looking at the values from B3 to B5, E6 from B4 to B6, and that goes the same all the way down to cell E13 and the three month running average for December looks at cells B11 through B13.
>
> **[6:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-running-averages-24045729?u=76281980&t=368)** As you can see, you have a lot of ways to summarize your data.
>
> **[6:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-running-averages-24045729?u=76281980&t=371)** Running total, running average, and three month running average are three very popular measures for business data.

### 3. Testing a Hypothesis

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### Formulate a hypothesis
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/formulate-a-hypothesis-24047698?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/formulate-a-hypothesis-24047698?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When you analyze your data using statistics, you should know what aspect of your data you want to test.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/formulate-a-hypothesis-24047698?u=76281980&t=6)** The statement that embodies your test is called a hypothesis.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/formulate-a-hypothesis-24047698?u=76281980&t=11)** Your hypothesis is an educated guess about the characteristics of a data set and the circumstance it describes.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/formulate-a-hypothesis-24047698?u=76281980&t=18)** For example, you could create a hypothesis that says the state a customer lives in is related to the amount of olive oil they order from your company.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/formulate-a-hypothesis-24047698?u=76281980&t=27)** There are two parts of a hypothesis used for hypothesis testing.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/formulate-a-hypothesis-24047698?u=76281980&t=32)** The first is the null hypothesis, which simply says that factor A has no effect on B.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/formulate-a-hypothesis-24047698?u=76281980&t=38)** In the example I gave earlier, it would say that the state a customer lives in has no effect on how much they spend at my store.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/formulate-a-hypothesis-24047698?u=76281980&t=46)** The alternative hypothesis says factor A affects B.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/formulate-a-hypothesis-24047698?u=76281980&t=50)** So the state that a customer lives in does affect how much they spend at my store.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/formulate-a-hypothesis-24047698?u=76281980&t=56)** An alternative hypothesis can be directional or non-directional.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/formulate-a-hypothesis-24047698?u=76281980&t=60)** A directional hypothesis looks at either a value that is greater than or less than a target.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/formulate-a-hypothesis-24047698?u=76281980&t=67)** For example, customers who were part of a specific marketing group might spend more than the average based on the effect of that advertising.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/formulate-a-hypothesis-24047698?u=76281980&t=79)** Whereas customers who were previous customers but had not ordered for awhile would spend less.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/formulate-a-hypothesis-24047698?u=76281980&t=85)** A non-directional alternative hypothesis looks for a comparison to a specific value.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/formulate-a-hypothesis-24047698?u=76281980&t=92)** You might say that 30% of customers who ordered one product would order another.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/formulate-a-hypothesis-24047698?u=76281980&t=97)** And you're looking for variation either above or below.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/formulate-a-hypothesis-24047698?u=76281980&t=101)** So it can be both instead of just one.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/formulate-a-hypothesis-24047698?u=76281980&t=105)** So how do you create an effective alternative hypothesis?
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/formulate-a-hypothesis-24047698?u=76281980&t=108)** Well first, you need to state that there is a relationship between the variables, and then base your alternative on your knowledge of the world.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/formulate-a-hypothesis-24047698?u=76281980&t=116)** For example, if you believe that your advertising campaign will make a difference to sales, you need to state that.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/formulate-a-hypothesis-24047698?u=76281980&t=122)** You should express your hypothesis simply and briefly and also make sure you can test your alternative hypothesis.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/formulate-a-hypothesis-24047698?u=76281980&t=129)** Your hypothesis needs to be based on data that you are able to collect and that you believe to be reliable so that you can perform an accurate test.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** data (4), [[Statistics]] (1), product (1), express (1)
> **Definitions:** is called (1), is an  (1), is a  (1)
> **Analogies:** for example (3)
> **CLI Commands:** make (2)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### Interpret the results of your analysis
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-the-results-of-your-analysis-24046709?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-the-results-of-your-analysis-24046709?u=76281980&t=0)** - Businesses run on data, which is both good news and bad news.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-the-results-of-your-analysis-24046709?u=76281980&t=4)** The good news is that it has never been easier to collect data about your customers, products and sales.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-the-results-of-your-analysis-24046709?u=76281980&t=10)** The bad news is that you still have to decide what all that data means.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-the-results-of-your-analysis-24046709?u=76281980&t=15)** As an example, suppose you ran a web advertising campaign with a goal of bringing in at least 70 new qualified customers per week.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-the-results-of-your-analysis-24046709?u=76281980&t=24)** You ran the ads on 25 different sites for four weeks, so you have a sample size of 100.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-the-results-of-your-analysis-24046709?u=76281980&t=30)** On average, each ad brought in 73 new customers with a standard deviation of 15.1.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-the-results-of-your-analysis-24046709?u=76281980&t=38)** The central question is though whether the ads met your goal of bringing in 70 new customers with each campaign.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-the-results-of-your-analysis-24046709?u=76281980&t=44)** Your sample size is 100, and your mean is 73, but the standard deviation is pretty large, over 15.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-the-results-of-your-analysis-24046709?u=76281980&t=51)** So you have to ask yourself one question, did I just get lucky?
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-the-results-of-your-analysis-24046709?u=76281980&t=55)** I've used the numbers I collected to calculate my standard error and what's called a Z-score.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-the-results-of-your-analysis-24046709?u=76281980&t=61)** The Z-score lets me calculate how likely it is my results were due to chance, and in this case, again, based on all my values, the probability is 2.35%.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-the-results-of-your-analysis-24046709?u=76281980&t=75)** And 2.35%, okay, is that good? Bad?
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-the-results-of-your-analysis-24046709?u=76281980&t=80)** Neither?
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-the-results-of-your-analysis-24046709?u=76281980&t=81)** Most analysts say that a result is significant if the probability your results were due to chance is below 5%.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-the-results-of-your-analysis-24046709?u=76281980&t=88)** In other words, if your data would show that the conclusion will be wrong less than one time out of 20, then you can claim the result is significant.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-the-results-of-your-analysis-24046709?u=76281980&t=98)** So in this case, 97.65%, or 100% minus 2.35, appears to be significant past the 95% confidence level.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-the-results-of-your-analysis-24046709?u=76281980&t=109)** Of course, there is still the possibility your results actually were due to chance and that your ads did not have a meaningful effect.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-the-results-of-your-analysis-24046709?u=76281980&t=116)** That's where your knowledge of your business, intuition, and willingness to continue analyzing comes in handy.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** data (4), [[Probability]] (2), [[Microsoft Products|Products]] (1), web (1), business (1)
> **Versions:** 2.35 (3), 15.1 (1), 97.65 (1)
> **Definitions:** in other words (1)
> **Speakers:** - businesses (1)

#### Consider the limits of hypothesis testing
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/consider-the-limits-of-hypothesis-testing-24048669?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/consider-the-limits-of-hypothesis-testing-24048669?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] As much as we'd like to think that statistics is an exact science, in fact, there's a lot of room for uncertainty.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/consider-the-limits-of-hypothesis-testing-24048669?u=76281980&t=6)** In this movie, I want to go over some of the limits of hypothesis testing to give you some cautions about your data and your analysis.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/consider-the-limits-of-hypothesis-testing-24048669?u=76281980&t=15)** The first is that even if your analysis meets the 95% standard that most analysts use, you will still be wrong one out of 20 times.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/consider-the-limits-of-hypothesis-testing-24048669?u=76281980&t=24)** And if you've ever performed any type of analysis or even flipped a coin and gotten four or five tails in a row, then you know how likely it is that events can happen one out of 16, one out of 20, or even one out of 100 times.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/consider-the-limits-of-hypothesis-testing-24048669?u=76281980&t=38)** Also, you might run into errors in data collection or analysis that can skew your results.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/consider-the-limits-of-hypothesis-testing-24048669?u=76281980&t=44)** Some errors in data collection are not randomized in your samples or using old data, so make sure that your data is collected properly and that it isn't too old and that circumstances haven't changed to make the analysis invalid.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/consider-the-limits-of-hypothesis-testing-24048669?u=76281980&t=60)** And also remember that correlation is not causation.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/consider-the-limits-of-hypothesis-testing-24048669?u=76281980&t=63)** Just because two things happen together doesn't mean that one caused the other.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/consider-the-limits-of-hypothesis-testing-24048669?u=76281980&t=69)** That said, correlation does tell you where to start looking.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/consider-the-limits-of-hypothesis-testing-24048669?u=76281980&t=73)** If two things happen together and it's plausible that there is a way that one could have caused the other, then that's a great place to start looking.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/consider-the-limits-of-hypothesis-testing-24048669?u=76281980&t=82)** As always, this is where your intuition and knowledge of the business will help out a lot.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** data (5), [[Statistics]] (1), business (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (2)
> **Definitions:** is an  (1), is a  (1)
> **Speakers:** - [narrator] (1)

#### Challenge: Formulate and test a hypothesis
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/challenge-formulate-and-test-a-hypothesis-24043672?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/challenge-formulate-and-test-a-hypothesis-24043672?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In this chapter, I have described how to formulate a hypothesis and also given an overview of how to test it.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/challenge-formulate-and-test-a-hypothesis-24043672?u=76281980&t=12)** As a reminder, a hypothesis is an educated guess about a relationship between two values in your dataset.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/challenge-formulate-and-test-a-hypothesis-24043672?u=76281980&t=20)** The null hypothesis says that factor A has no effect on factor B, and the alternative hypothesis says that in fact factor A does affect factor B.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/challenge-formulate-and-test-a-hypothesis-24043672?u=76281980&t=31)** The alternative hypothesis can be directional, where you look for less than or greater than, or non-directional where you're looking for a difference of any kind up or down from a particular value.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/challenge-formulate-and-test-a-hypothesis-24043672?u=76281980&t=43)** So in this movie, I would like to set a challenge, which is to create an alternative hypothesis.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/challenge-formulate-and-test-a-hypothesis-24043672?u=76281980&t=48)** You want to know if an ad campaign was effective, and the question is, did sales for ad recipients increase by at least 10%?
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/challenge-formulate-and-test-a-hypothesis-24043672?u=76281980&t=59)** So what I'd like you to do is to create a null hypothesis.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/challenge-formulate-and-test-a-hypothesis-24043672?u=76281980&t=63)** Create an alternative hypothesis that answers the question that we're testing for, and then identify if your hypothesis is one tailed or two tailed, and whether you can test it.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/challenge-formulate-and-test-a-hypothesis-24043672?u=76281980&t=77)** In the next movie, I will show you one way to approach this problem.

#### Solution: Formulate and test a hypothesis

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>

>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-formulate-and-test-a-hypothesis-24045733?u=76281980&t=11)** You want to know if an ad campaign was effective.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-formulate-and-test-a-hypothesis-24045733?u=76281980&t=13)** And the question was whether sales for ad recipients increased by at least 10%?
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-formulate-and-test-a-hypothesis-24045733?u=76281980&t=21)** I invited you to create a null hypothesis, an alternative hypothesis that examines the question in the second point.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-formulate-and-test-a-hypothesis-24045733?u=76281980&t=29)** And then also identify if your hypothesis is one tailed or two tailed, and whether you can test it.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-formulate-and-test-a-hypothesis-24045733?u=76281980&t=36)** So here's how I might approach the problem.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-formulate-and-test-a-hypothesis-24045733?u=76281980&t=39)** The null hypothesis says advertising did not increase sales by 10% or more.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-formulate-and-test-a-hypothesis-24045733?u=76281980&t=45)** In other words, there was no effect.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-formulate-and-test-a-hypothesis-24045733?u=76281980&t=49)** The alternative hypothesis says advertising increased sales by 10% or more.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-formulate-and-test-a-hypothesis-24045733?u=76281980&t=54)** Also, this alternative hypothesis is directional.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-formulate-and-test-a-hypothesis-24045733?u=76281980&t=58)** We are testing for an increase.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-formulate-and-test-a-hypothesis-24045733?u=76281980&t=61)** And it can be tested by analyzing sales data from ad viewers.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-formulate-and-test-a-hypothesis-24045733?u=76281980&t=65)** As long as you are collecting the data that you need, you'll be able to identify individuals who saw the ads and whether or not they're spending increased over the time period that you were measuring.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-formulate-and-test-a-hypothesis-24045733?u=76281980&t=77)** I hope you had fun with this challenge and that it prepares you to ask interesting and useful questions about your data.

### 4. Utilizing Data Distributions

#### Use the normal distribution
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] The data distribution that you are most likely to use during your analysis is the normal distribution.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=6)** The normal curve, or bell curve, has the shape shown in this chart.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=11)** This chart indicates probabilities for a curve with an average or mean of 100 and a standard deviation of 20.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=18)** The mean is usually indicated by the Greek letter Mu, and the standard deviation by the Greek letter Sigma.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=25)** The normal curve has some very useful properties.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=29)** The first is that approximately 68% of all values will occur within plus or minus one standard deviation.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=37)** So with our mean of 100, that would mean that about 68% of the values would fall within 20 above or below the average.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=46)** So 80 to 120.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=49)** 95% of values will be within two standard deviations, so 60 to 140.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=55)** And 99.7% within three standard deviations, plus or minus.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=60)** So that's 40 to 160.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=63)** To see how to work with these values in Excel, I'll switch over to our practice workbook.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=68)** I've switched over to Excel and my sample file is 0401 normal, and you can find it in the chapter four folder of the exercise files collection.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=78)** I use the values and columns A and B to create the graph of the curve that you see at the bottom right.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=85)** But let's ask some numerical questions of our data.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=88)** For example, we can calculate the probability of getting exactly 92.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=93)** So we have an average of 100, standard deviation of 20, 92 is close to the middle.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=99)** So let's calculate the probability of getting exactly that value if we're generating random numbers.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=106)** So I'll click in cell E1 and then type in equals sign.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=112)** And the function we use is norm.dist.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=115)** And as you might guess, that stands for normal distribution.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=120)** The value we're working with our X is 92, so I'll type that in, then a comma.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=125)** The mean is in B1.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=126)** Comma.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=127)** Standard deviation in B2, then a comma.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=131)** And we are looking for the probability mass function, which is also called a point probability.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=137)** And that means that for the last argument, I need to select false.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=141)** So I highlight that.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=143)** Press tab to accept it, type a right parentheses and enter.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=148)** And we see the probability of getting exactly 92 as 1.84%.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=153)** And that might seem pretty low, but remember within three standard deviations, we go from 40 to 160.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=159)** So the fact that 92 is as probable as it is at a random selection is an indication of how close to the average it is.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=170)** Now let's calculate the probability of getting 92 or more, and I will do it incorrectly the first time and then show you how to fix what is a very common mistake.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=179)** So in E2 I'll type equal.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=182)** Norm.dist.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=184)** As before, our X is 92.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=186)** The mean is in B1, standard deviation in B2 and then a comma.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=191)** But now we do want to look for the cumulative distribution function, and that's because we're looking for 92 or more.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=198)** So we want to spread of values instead of a single point probability.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=203)** So I highlight true, type a right parentheses, and again, this is going to be an incorrect result.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=210)** I get 34.46 of getting 92 or more.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=215)** And here's why that's wrong.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=217)** 92 is to the left of the mean.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=222)** And if you look at the normal curve, half the values are greater than the mean and the other half are less than the mean.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=229)** So the fact that our calculation shows only 34.46% of values are greater than 92, which is less than the mean, must be incorrect.
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=240)** The way to fix this error is to subtract that calculation from one.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=245)** So I will double click in cell E2.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=249)** And then I will add one minus our previous calculation.
>
> **[4:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=254)** Now when I press tab, I get 65.54%, and that makes a lot more sense because 92 is approximately here.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=263)** I've highlighted 90.
>
> **[4:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=266)** And I'll just leave the mouse pointer there to show you the approximate point.
>
> **[4:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=270)** You can see that about 65.54% of the values are to the right.
>
> **[4:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=275)** So our calculation makes intuitive sense.
>
> **[4:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=278)** We can also ask about percentages of values within a distribution.
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=282)** So let's say what is the value inside this curve or as part of this data distribution that 33% of values are below.
>
> **[4:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=291)** So I will click in cell H1 and type in equals sign.
>
> **[4:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=296)** We can't use norm dis for this calculation, but we can use a different function.
>
> **[5:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=301)** Norm.inv.
>
> **[5:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=304)** And this is the inverse of the normal distribution.
>
> **[5:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=307)** Where we got probabilities with norm.dist, the inverse of that gives us values based on a probability.
>
> **[5:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=316)** So our probability is 33%, and then a comma.
>
> **[5:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=322)** Our mean is still in B1, standard deviation is still in B2.
>
> **[5:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=325)** We don't need any other arguments.
>
> **[5:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=327)** So I'll type A right parentheses and enter, and we get 91.2.
>
> **[5:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=333)** And this again makes sense.
>
> **[5:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=336)** About 33% of our values are below 91, which again is here on the curve, approximately.
>
> **[5:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=343)** And that will show that about 33% of the values are to the left.
>
> **[5:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=349)** So our value checks out.
>
> **[5:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=351)** If I want to find the value for which 90% of values in this curve are above, then I can do norm.inv.
>
> **[6:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=360)** And if you're suspecting that we need to do one minus something, as we did with probability of 92 or more, you are correct, but we put it in a different place.
>
> **[6:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=369)** So in H2 I'll type an equals sign.
>
> **[6:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=372)** Norm.inv.
>
> **[6:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=374)** The result we would get by typing in 90% would be to return a value that 90% of values are below.
>
> **[6:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=381)** So we need to subtract the percentage from one as part of the probability calculation.
>
> **[6:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=387)** So for the first argument, I'll type one minus 90%, then a comma.
>
> **[6:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=394)** B1 for the mean.
>
> **[6:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=395)** B2 for the standard deviation.
>
> **[6:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=397)** Right parentheses and enter.
>
> **[6:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=399)** And we get 74.37 approximately.
>
> **[6:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=403)** And again, that makes sense.
>
> **[6:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=405)** If I go down to 74.
>
> **[6:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=408)** That's 72.
>
> **[6:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=410)** No, there's 74.
>
> **[6:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=412)** We can see where it lies on the curve.
>
> **[6:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=414)** And it makes sense that about 90% of values, including the fat part of the curve in the middle, would be above the return value of about 74.37.
>
> **[7:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-normal-distribution-24050673?u=76281980&t=427)** So as you can see, you can do a lot with the normal curve, especially with the functions norm.dist and norm.inv.
