#### Use a uniform distribution
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-a-uniform-distribution-24043681?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-a-uniform-distribution-24043681?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Presenter] Many business cases analyze events that are not equally likely.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-a-uniform-distribution-24043681?u=76281980&t=4)** Some products are more popular than others, and your website gets more visitors in certain parts of the day and less than others.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-a-uniform-distribution-24043681?u=76281980&t=11)** In this movie, I will show you how to generate random values when all of the events are equally likely, such as in a random drawing.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-a-uniform-distribution-24043681?u=76281980&t=19)** This is called the uniform distribution, and if there are four possible outcomes, values one through four, then in the uniform distribution, the probability of each would be 25%.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-a-uniform-distribution-24043681?u=76281980&t=31)** Now, over time, it is very unlikely that you will have the same number of values exactly, however, they will be close, and here is one case where I did a random generation of numbers from one to four, and here, you see the actual results.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-a-uniform-distribution-24043681?u=76281980&t=50)** So as with most things in life, theory and practice are often close, but do divert.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-a-uniform-distribution-24043681?u=76281980&t=56)** Now let's switch over to Excel, and I will show you how to work with values that are uniformly distributed.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-a-uniform-distribution-24043681?u=76281980&t=63)** The Excel workbook that I'm using is 04_02_Uniform, and you can find it in the chapter four folder of the exercise files collection.

>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-a-uniform-distribution-24043681?u=76281980&t=78)** Let's say that I want to have a minimum value of one, so I'll type that in cell B3 then enter, and a maximum value of six, so type six and enter.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-a-uniform-distribution-24043681?u=76281980&t=91)** And our number of values I know is six, but if I want to calculate it, I can do equal B4 minus B3 plus one, and that's because I am subtracting the value in B3, which in this case is one, so I need to add one back, otherwise I would get five.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-a-uniform-distribution-24043681?u=76281980&t=111)** So I'll press enter and enter again to move down to cell B7.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-a-uniform-distribution-24043681?u=76281980&t=117)** If I want to generate a random number that is a random whole number or integer between one and six, then I can use the function RANDBETWEEN.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-a-uniform-distribution-24043681?u=76281980&t=129)** In B7 I will type equal, and then the function is RANDBETWEEN, and the bottom value is in B3, top value in B4, right parentheses and enter, and I get the value of three.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-a-uniform-distribution-24043681?u=76281980&t=147)** Now for value two, I will do the same thing.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-a-uniform-distribution-24043681?u=76281980&t=149)** So I'll do equal RANDBETWEEN, excuse me, I started typing in the numbers directly, but it's better to use a cell reference so you can change the values without having to edit a formula, so that's B3 from the bottom value, and then comma, top value's in B4, right parentheses and enter, and my worksheet recalculated, so I have both four and three.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-a-uniform-distribution-24043681?u=76281980&t=175)** Now I can calculate the probability of getting value one, so I will go over to cell E3.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-a-uniform-distribution-24043681?u=76281980&t=184)** And here, because I know that I have a uniform distribution, that every value is equally likely, so I will in cell E3 type an equal sign, then one, because I'm only looking for one value, and divide that by the number of values, which is in cell B5.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-a-uniform-distribution-24043681?u=76281980&t=203)** Enter, and again a probability of 0.166 repeating, so one sixth of the time, which matches our six possible values.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-a-uniform-distribution-24043681?u=76281980&t=214)** If I want to calculate the probability of value one or value two, then that would be equal one divided by the number of values, which again is in B5, plus one divided by B5 again, because I'm looking at two distinct values.
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-a-uniform-distribution-24043681?u=76281980&t=231)** Enter, and I get 0.33, and yes, it is possible that the randomly generated values in cells B7 and B8 could be the same, in which case the probability of value one or value two would be the same as getting a single value, but I'm working in the abstract here as opposed to with these specific values in B7 and B8.
>
> **[4:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-a-uniform-distribution-24043681?u=76281980&t=255)** If you want to calculate the probability of getting neither value one or value two, then you would subtract the probability of the positive outcome from one, so in cell E7 I'll type equal, and it'll be one minus the value in E5.
>
> **[4:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-a-uniform-distribution-24043681?u=76281980&t=272)** Enter, and we get 0.666 repeating, so about two thirds of the time, which makes sense.
>
> **[4:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-a-uniform-distribution-24043681?u=76281980&t=279)** Four out of six times you will not get one or two values, distinct values, and that will happen about two thirds of the time, so our value is correct.
>
> **[4:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-a-uniform-distribution-24043681?u=76281980&t=290)** And I have saved cell B10 for last, because I want to show you another way to generate random numbers between zero and one, and this is a 16 digit decimal.
>
> **[5:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-a-uniform-distribution-24043681?u=76281980&t=302)** So in B10, I'll type equal and then rand followed by an open and closed parentheses, and this value will be a random number, a decimal number between zero and one.
>
> **[5:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-a-uniform-distribution-24043681?u=76281980&t=315)** So enter, and there we get 0.81534.
>
> **[5:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-a-uniform-distribution-24043681?u=76281980&t=320)** And the number is actually larger than that, so if I click cell B10 and go to the home tab and click increase decimal, you can see that the value increases.
>
> **[5:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-a-uniform-distribution-24043681?u=76281980&t=335)** So what is the probability of generating this specific value?
>
> **[5:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-a-uniform-distribution-24043681?u=76281980&t=341)** It is effectively zero.
>
> **[5:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-a-uniform-distribution-24043681?u=76281980&t=343)** There are so many decimal points here, and I'm just estimating, let's say there are 12 digits there.
>
> **[5:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-a-uniform-distribution-24043681?u=76281980&t=350)** If we look at 12 digits or 13, that would be in the billions, so the probability of getting exactly this value is extremely low.
>
> **[6:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-a-uniform-distribution-24043681?u=76281980&t=361)** And if I press F9 to recalculate, I get a different value, and again, and the probability of those values repeating is so small, it's not worth worrying about.
>
> **[6:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-a-uniform-distribution-24043681?u=76281980&t=372)** So I hope that this movie has given you an introduction to working with random numbers, both as integers and as decimal values between zero and one.

#### Use the exponential distribution
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-exponential-distribution-24046706?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-exponential-distribution-24046706?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] One of the most important pieces of information you can learn about your business operations is how long it takes to perform a specific task.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-exponential-distribution-24046706?u=76281980&t=8)** For example, if you repair phone screens, you might find that the average time to repair is five minutes.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-exponential-distribution-24046706?u=76281980&t=16)** You can use the exponential distribution using that average of five, denoted here by LAMBDA, as part of the exponential distribution to determine the probability of a specific time.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-exponential-distribution-24046706?u=76281980&t=30)** This is what the graph looks like, and it may seem odd that the average time of five only occurs about 7% of the time.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-exponential-distribution-24046706?u=76281980&t=39)** And the reason that's the case is because there is the possibility of long times, for example, 20 or 25 minutes.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-exponential-distribution-24046706?u=76281980&t=47)** However, those long duration repairs are offset by the much higher probability of having a repair taking one, two, three, four, or five minutes.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-exponential-distribution-24046706?u=76281980&t=58)** To see how this works in Excel, I will switch over to our sample workbook.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-exponential-distribution-24046706?u=76281980&t=64)** I have opened our Excel sample file and that is 04_03_Exponential.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-exponential-distribution-24046706?u=76281980&t=69)** You can find that in the Chapter 4 folder of the Exercise Files collection.

>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-exponential-distribution-24046706?u=76281980&t=88)** So I'll click in cell B2, type in equal sign, and I will use the exponential distribution function, that is EXPON.DIST.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-exponential-distribution-24046706?u=76281980&t=100)** First, I need to know my X, that is the value that I'm comparing.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-exponential-distribution-24046706?u=76281980&t=104)** So that's in cell A2 for this particular calculation, then the comma.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-exponential-distribution-24046706?u=76281980&t=109)** The LAMBDA is in cell E1, but I need to use 1 divided by LAMBDA, and that is a characteristic of the exponential distribution.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-exponential-distribution-24046706?u=76281980&t=119)** If you don't divide 1 by LAMBDA, you will get very strange results.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-exponential-distribution-24046706?u=76281980&t=124)** So in cell B2, I'll type 1 divided by E1, and because I want this value to remain constant, the cell reference remain constant.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-exponential-distribution-24046706?u=76281980&t=136)** I'll press F4 on Windows, it's Command-T on the Mac, then a comma.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-exponential-distribution-24046706?u=76281980&t=144)** And I am looking for the probability density function or point probability.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-exponential-distribution-24046706?u=76281980&t=149)** So I will highlight false for this final argument, press tab, write parentheses, and enter.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-exponential-distribution-24046706?u=76281980&t=156)** And I get a probability of about 0.1637.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-exponential-distribution-24046706?u=76281980&t=162)** I can now copy this formula down to the remaining cells.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-exponential-distribution-24046706?u=76281980&t=166)** So I will click cell B2 and then double click the fill handle at the bottom right corner.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-exponential-distribution-24046706?u=76281980&t=172)** I know that my mouse pointer is in the right place when it changes to a black cross.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-exponential-distribution-24046706?u=76281980&t=177)** Double click, and can see that the formula has been copied down and I see the individual probabilities.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-exponential-distribution-24046706?u=76281980&t=185)** Now, let's say that I want to calculate the probability of getting a value between 3 and 7.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-exponential-distribution-24046706?u=76281980&t=193)** So I have already calculated my probabilities for 3 here and then 7 here.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-exponential-distribution-24046706?u=76281980&t=200)** But because they're point probabilities, I can't use them.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-exponential-distribution-24046706?u=76281980&t=203)** I need to use the cumulative function.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-exponential-distribution-24046706?u=76281980&t=206)** So I will go to cell E3 and then type in equal sign.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-exponential-distribution-24046706?u=76281980&t=212)** And I'm going to subtract the probability of getting 3 or less from the probability of getting 7 or less.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-exponential-distribution-24046706?u=76281980&t=220)** So I'll start by calculating the value for 7.
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-exponential-distribution-24046706?u=76281980&t=224)** That is the cumulative probability of getting 7 or less.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-exponential-distribution-24046706?u=76281980&t=227)** So that's EXPON.DIST.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-exponential-distribution-24046706?u=76281980&t=232)** The value is 7, LAMBDA, and again, it needs to be 1 divided by LAMBDA.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-exponential-distribution-24046706?u=76281980&t=238)** That's an E1.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-exponential-distribution-24046706?u=76281980&t=239)** I'm not going to copy the formula, so I won't worry about a cell reference, and it is cumulative.
>
> **[4:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-exponential-distribution-24046706?u=76281980&t=244)** So I will highlight true, which it already is, and then press tab to get that.
>
> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-exponential-distribution-24046706?u=76281980&t=251)** Then write parentheses and I will subtract the same calculation for the number 3.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-exponential-distribution-24046706?u=76281980&t=256)** I'll go through it a little bit more quickly.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-exponential-distribution-24046706?u=76281980&t=259)** Exponential distribution 3, 1 divided by E1, cumulative true, write parentheses, and enter, and I get 30.22%.
>
> **[4:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-exponential-distribution-24046706?u=76281980&t=273)** So the probability of getting a value between 3 and 7 inclusive is 30.22% or about a third of the time.
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-exponential-distribution-24046706?u=76281980&t=280)** And that makes sense with an average of five.

#### Use the Poisson distribution
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-poisson-distribution-24043680?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-poisson-distribution-24043680?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] If you analyze business data, and especially if you perform any kind of simulation, it's useful to know about the Poisson distribution.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-poisson-distribution-24043680?u=76281980&t=9)** In this movie, I will describe the characteristics of that distribution and also show you how to use it in Excel.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-poisson-distribution-24043680?u=76281980&t=16)** The Poisson distribution uses an average, called Lambda, and it determines the amount of time between events occurring.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-poisson-distribution-24043680?u=76281980&t=25)** So for example, if you have customers that arrive on average every seven minutes, then this is what the curve would look like given that your data is distributed according to the Poisson distribution.

>

>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-poisson-distribution-24043680?u=76281980&t=228)** And then I will click cell B2, double-click the fill handle.

>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-poisson-distribution-24043680?u=76281980&t=237)** So as you can see, it's rare that we will get values between zero and five.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-poisson-distribution-24043680?u=76281980&t=241)** Those will happen about a quarter of the time.
>
> **[4:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-poisson-distribution-24043680?u=76281980&t=244)** And then up to 10, because remember our average is seven, we have a probability cumulatively of 0.9.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-poisson-distribution-24043680?u=76281980&t=253)** And then as we get further out, the values get closer and closer to one.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-poisson-distribution-24043680?u=76281980&t=259)** Two final things to note about the Poisson distribution.
>
> **[4:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-poisson-distribution-24043680?u=76281980&t=262)** The first is that it only deals with whole numbers, so you won't get any decimals in there.
>
> **[4:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-poisson-distribution-24043680?u=76281980&t=268)** And secondly, you can think of it as the inverse of the exponential function.
>
> **[4:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-poisson-distribution-24043680?u=76281980&t=273)** Remember, when we used the exponential function, we needed to divide one by Lambda.
>
> **[4:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-poisson-distribution-24043680?u=76281980&t=279)** In the Poisson distribution we use Lambda by itself.

#### Use the binomial distribution

> **[4:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-binomial-distribution-24044688?u=76281980&t=267)** And that continues on up to 20.
>
> **[4:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-binomial-distribution-24044688?u=76281980&t=270)** And you can see that there is not much of a change in the probability between getting 14, so that's 96.97% and 15, which is 99.995%.
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-binomial-distribution-24044688?u=76281980&t=284)** So it is very unlikely that we will have anything more than about 10 people showing up or signing up in a particular hour.
>
> **[4:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-binomial-distribution-24044688?u=76281980&t=294)** Analyzing data, using the binomial distribution lets you determine the likelihood of achieving certain results when faced with a series of yes or no trials.
>
> **[5:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/use-the-binomial-distribution-24044688?u=76281980&t=303)** If you're not hitting your targets for manual signup or sales opportunities, you should consider whether your approach needs to be changed or if your expected success rate is too high.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>

> **[7:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-model-operational-data-using-distributions-24046707?u=76281980&t=478)** That's pretty good.
>

### 5. Measuring Covariance and Correlation

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### Visualize what covariance means
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-covariance-means-24048670?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-covariance-means-24048670?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When you analyze data, it is often important to see how two sets of values vary in relation to one another.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-covariance-means-24048670?u=76281980&t=7)** One way to do that is to calculate the covariance of the data sets.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-covariance-means-24048670?u=76281980&t=11)** The covariance formula looks a little intimidating, but I'll break it down for you step by step.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-covariance-means-24048670?u=76281980&t=18)** If you have two sets of values in columns, you can find the average of each column and the average's indicated by a bar above the variable name.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-covariance-means-24048670?u=76281980&t=27)** So you see x bar, which is the average of all the x values, column one, and y bar is the average or mean of all of the y values.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-covariance-means-24048670?u=76281980&t=35)** That would be your second column.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-covariance-means-24048670?u=76281980&t=38)** Then to average the covariance of a pair of values, you subtract the mean of column one, from the first value in column one, and subtract the mean of column two, from the first value of column two, and then multiply those values together, for each pair of data points.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-covariance-means-24048670?u=76281980&t=54)** You find the sum of all of those values and then finally, you divide by the number of data pairs.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-covariance-means-24048670?u=76281980&t=60)** So if you have 10 pairs of values, you would divide that sum by 10, and the result is given in terms of the original data, such as dollars per mile driven, or perhaps customers versus dollar spent.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-covariance-means-24048670?u=76281980&t=75)** The next question is how you interpret covariance values.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-covariance-means-24048670?u=76281980&t=78)** If the value is zero, which is rare, but can happen, the data sets don't vary together at all.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-covariance-means-24048670?u=76281980&t=85)** If you have a positive covariance, the data sets tend to move in the same direction.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-covariance-means-24048670?u=76281980&t=90)** So if one value goes up, such as personal income, then the other value would go up, such as amount spent at your store.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-covariance-means-24048670?u=76281980&t=98)** If the values show a negative covariance, then they tend to move in opposite directions.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-covariance-means-24048670?u=76281980&t=104)** Finally then, you can ask, is my covariance significant?
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-covariance-means-24048670?u=76281980&t=108)** And I have to admit that this is a difficult question to answer.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-covariance-means-24048670?u=76281980&t=113)** In general, values close to zero do indicate little relationship, and also, large positive or negative values can be significant.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-covariance-means-24048670?u=76281980&t=122)** So look at the covariance in relation to the means of each data set.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-covariance-means-24048670?u=76281980&t=127)** If you have a positive covariance of 500 and a mean of 100, then the covariance is five times greater than the mean, and that might be significant, but that kind of analysis does come down to individual interpretation.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-covariance-means-24048670?u=76281980&t=143)** In practice, what most analysts do is convert covariances to correlations.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-covariance-means-24048670?u=76281980&t=149)** But before we take that step, I will show you how to calculate covariance for different sets of values.

#### Calculate covariance among multiple pairs of columns
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-covariance-among-multiple-pairs-of-columns-24043675?u=76281980)

#### Visualize what correlation means

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-correlation-means-24043683?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When you analyze business data, you will often want to know whether two sets of values are related.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-correlation-means-24043683?u=76281980&t=6)** One way to do that is to calculate correlation.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-correlation-means-24043683?u=76281980&t=10)** Previously, I showed you how to calculate core variance, and as a reminder, here is that formula.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-correlation-means-24043683?u=76281980&t=16)** The idea is that you multiply the differences from the mean for each value pair.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-correlation-means-24043683?u=76281980&t=22)** So we have two columns of data, and we have an X and Y value that are matched up.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-correlation-means-24043683?u=76281980&t=27)** And you find the sum of that product.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-correlation-means-24043683?u=76281980&t=31)** You then divide that total by the number of data pairs.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-correlation-means-24043683?u=76281980&t=35)** Correlation is related, and here is the formula.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-correlation-means-24043683?u=76281980&t=39)** So you'll see that the top term is the same, but it's divided by the term shown.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-correlation-means-24043683?u=76281980&t=44)** And it takes a while to explain, so I will just ask you to accept the term as given and use it in your calculations.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-correlation-means-24043683?u=76281980&t=54)** So the next question is how do you interpret your correlation values?
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-correlation-means-24043683?u=76281980&t=58)** You will get a value between one and negative one.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-correlation-means-24043683?u=76281980&t=61)** Data that is completely uncorrelated returns a correlation value of zero.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-correlation-means-24043683?u=76281980&t=67)** In other words, the two sets of values have nothing to do with each other.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-correlation-means-24043683?u=76281980&t=71)** Data that is positively correlated will be between zero and one.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-correlation-means-24043683?u=76281980&t=76)** And if you have one, that means the data sets move in lockstep.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-correlation-means-24043683?u=76281980&t=81)** In other words, they move the same way all the time, every time.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-correlation-means-24043683?u=76281980&t=87)** And if you have data that is negatively correlated between minus one and zero, then the data moves in opposite directions.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-correlation-means-24043683?u=76281980&t=94)** So when one set of values goes up, the other one goes down.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-correlation-means-24043683?u=76281980&t=99)** And of course, it's possible to have values between zero and one or zero and negative one, and that means the correlation isn't quite as strong.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-correlation-means-24043683?u=76281980&t=108)** So let's take a look at a visual example of data that is not correlated.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-correlation-means-24043683?u=76281980&t=113)** Here I have five starting values, and those are one, two, three, four and five.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-correlation-means-24043683?u=76281980&t=117)** You can see those on the horizontal axis, and one produces two results, three and negative three, and the same for the other values along the horizontal axis.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-correlation-means-24043683?u=76281980&t=128)** What this means is that the starting value tells you nothing about the value that follows it.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-correlation-means-24043683?u=76281980&t=132)** The X says nothing about the Y, so the correlation is zero.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-correlation-means-24043683?u=76281980&t=137)** If you have data with a perfect positive correlation, in other words, a correlation of one, then you'll see that it goes up and the values exactly match.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-correlation-means-24043683?u=76281980&t=147)** One gives you one, two, gives you two, and so on.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-correlation-means-24043683?u=76281980&t=150)** It doesn't have to be this exact pattern, but you can see a visual example of what a correlation of one looks like.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-correlation-means-24043683?u=76281980&t=158)** And data with a perfect negative correlation goes in the opposite direction.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-correlation-means-24043683?u=76281980&t=164)** The next question you have to ask, of course, is, is my correlation significant?
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-correlation-means-24043683?u=76281980&t=169)** And that depends on several factors.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-correlation-means-24043683?u=76281980&t=172)** You have the number of measurements, whether a value can be positive or negative, and by that, I mean if you're looking for a positive or negative value only, in other words, one side, then that's called a one-tailed test.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-correlation-means-24043683?u=76281980&t=186)** If your difference can be either positive or negative, then you have a two-tailed test.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-correlation-means-24043683?u=76281980&t=192)** And then you look up the correlation value in a table, which you can find in statistics textbooks or online, and see what that looks like.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-correlation-means-24043683?u=76281980&t=202)** And here is an example of a two-tailed correlation lookup table.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-correlation-means-24043683?u=76281980&t=207)** N, the leftmost column, gives you the number of samples that you have, and then to the right, you have different confidence levels.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-correlation-means-24043683?u=76281980&t=216)** And you subtract that confidence level from one to give you the value that you want.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-correlation-means-24043683?u=76281980&t=222)** So for example, in the column next to N, 0.1, you subtract that from one for the 90% confidence interval or a confidence level.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-correlation-means-24043683?u=76281980&t=232)** And then to the right of that, you have .05, so that's 95%, 98%, and the other two values you see there.
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-correlation-means-24043683?u=76281980&t=240)** So if you have 10 values and you calculate the correlation, and you want to look at the 90% level, then you would need to have a correlation value of at least .55.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-correlation-means-24043683?u=76281980&t=253)** And that's the value at the intersection of 10 and 0.1, to have a 90% certainty or 90% confidence level that the correlation is valid.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-correlation-means-24043683?u=76281980&t=265)** And the various other combinations are available here.
>
> **[4:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/visualize-what-correlation-means-24043683?u=76281980&t=270)** So again, there are a lot of moving parts to correlation, but it is a very useful way to look at your data.

#### Calculate the correlation between two columns of data

> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-calculate-correlations-between-columns-of-data-24044690?u=76281980&t=105)** And again, array one and array two.

> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-calculate-correlations-between-columns-of-data-24044690?u=76281980&t=108)** Array one is A2 through A12, then a comma, and then array two, B2 to B12.

> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-calculate-correlations-between-columns-of-data-24044690?u=76281980&t=116)** Right parentheses and Enter, and I get a correlation of customers and sales of 0.84, and that is very high.

### 6. Calculating Probabilities, Combinations, and Permutations

#### Calculate simple probabilities
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-simple-probabilities-24045730?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-simple-probabilities-24045730?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] One thing you can do in Microsoft Excel when analyzing business scenarios is calculate the probability that something will happen.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-simple-probabilities-24045730?u=76281980&t=8)** In this movie, I will give you a quick review of how to calculate simple probabilities.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-simple-probabilities-24045730?u=76281980&t=14)** In this table, I have a description of an outcome and then also the probabilities and odds that it will or will not happen.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-simple-probabilities-24045730?u=76281980&t=24)** To start, if you want to know the probability of rolling a three on a six-sided die, so in other words, you have one chance out of six, then you would divide one by six to get 16.67% of success.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-simple-probabilities-24045730?u=76281980&t=38)** You can also think of that outcome in terms of odds where you have five ways not to roll a three, one way to do it, so your odds are five to one against.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-simple-probabilities-24045730?u=76281980&t=50)** In the alternative, let's say that you don't want to roll a three on a six sided die.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-simple-probabilities-24045730?u=76281980&t=56)** So one, two, four, five or six would be good, a three would not.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-simple-probabilities-24045730?u=76281980&t=60)** In that case, you have five out of six chances of succeeding, that is not rolling a three.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-simple-probabilities-24045730?u=76281980&t=66)** So the probability of success is 83.33%, and you can also think of that in terms of odds as five to one in your favor.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-simple-probabilities-24045730?u=76281980&t=76)** Calculating simple probabilities is fairly straightforward, and you can create simple Excel formulas in your worksheets to calculate those odds if need be.

#### Calculate compound probabilities
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-compound-probabilities-24047696?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-compound-probabilities-24047696?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In the previous movie, I described how to calculate simple probabilities, that is the probability of a single trial.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-compound-probabilities-24047696?u=76281980&t=8)** In this movie, I will describe compound probabilities.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-compound-probabilities-24047696?u=76281980&t=12)** Compound probabilities assume that you want to analyze multiple independent trials.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-compound-probabilities-24047696?u=76281980&t=18)** And by independent, that means that the second trial or outcome, in other words, the second flip of a coin or a throw of a die, is independent of the first.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-compound-probabilities-24047696?u=76281980&t=30)** If you do have multiple independent trials, then you will multiply probabilities to get a compound probability.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-compound-probabilities-24047696?u=76281980&t=36)** So flipping a coin will end up on heads half the time.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-compound-probabilities-24047696?u=76281980&t=40)** So the probability of getting heads twice in a row, assuming that the coin is fair, would be 1/2 times 1/2 equals 1/4.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-compound-probabilities-24047696?u=76281980&t=50)** One easy mistake to make is to assume the compound probability after you've already started.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-compound-probabilities-24047696?u=76281980&t=57)** In other words, one of the trials has been done.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-compound-probabilities-24047696?u=76281980&t=60)** If you're flipping a coin, again, a fair coin with a one half probability of getting heads and you flip and get heads, the probability of flipping heads twice in a row is no longer one quarter, it is one half because you've already done it once, and all that matters is the second trial, which is 50% or 1/2 likely to end up on heads.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-compound-probabilities-24047696?u=76281980&t=85)** To visualize the probabilities, I have created a chart, and you can see that I have two separate trials in the rows.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-compound-probabilities-24047696?u=76281980&t=94)** In the first, we have independent outcomes rolling a die, and we're asking the probability of rolling a three on the first outcome and rolling a three again on the second one.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-compound-probabilities-24047696?u=76281980&t=107)** Assuming everything is independent, then we have one chance out of six, each of those trials to come up a three.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-compound-probabilities-24047696?u=76281980&t=114)** So that would be 1/6 times 1/6 equals 1/36.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-compound-probabilities-24047696?u=76281980&t=119)** So we have one chance out of 36 or 35 to one odds against.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-compound-probabilities-24047696?u=76281980&t=124)** We might also ask, what is the probability of rolling exactly a three on the first outcome and not rolling a three, in other words, getting any other number on the second outcome.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-compound-probabilities-24047696?u=76281980&t=135)** Well for that, we have our probability of 1/6 of rolling a three, and then we have five out of six numbers that would allow us to succeed in our scenario.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-compound-probabilities-24047696?u=76281980&t=146)** So we multiply 1/6 and 5/6 and get 5/36, and those numbers make sense.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-compound-probabilities-24047696?u=76281980&t=154)** If we look back at the first row and we see that we have one out of 36 of rolling a three, if we add that to five out of 36 in the second row where we don't roll a three, we get six out of 36, which is one out of six, and is the probability of rolling a three in the first place.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-compound-probabilities-24047696?u=76281980&t=173)** Compound probabilities can get a little tricky, but after you make sure that the results of your trials are independent, you can use multiplication to find the compound probability of the composite outcome you're looking for.

> **Definitions:** in other words (3), means that (1)

#### Calculate expected value
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-expected-value-24046708?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-expected-value-24046708?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When you analyze business scenarios, you will often want to calculate the expected value of an overall scenario with multiple possible outcomes.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-expected-value-24046708?u=76281980&t=10)** As an example, you might think that there would be three scenarios and you have assigned probabilities to each of them.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-expected-value-24046708?u=76281980&t=17)** So the first scenario you believe is 30% likely, the second scenario is 40%, and the third 30%.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-expected-value-24046708?u=76281980&t=25)** Each of those scenarios will lead to a value or outcome.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-expected-value-24046708?u=76281980&t=30)** So in scenario 1, the value is $5,000, positive 5,000.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-expected-value-24046708?u=76281980&t=34)** For scenario 2, it's 10,000.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-expected-value-24046708?u=76281980&t=36)** And for scenario 3, you would have a loss of $10,000.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-expected-value-24046708?u=76281980&t=41)** You can then calculate the expected value for each of these scenarios.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-expected-value-24046708?u=76281980&t=46)** To do that, you multiply the probability by the value.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-expected-value-24046708?u=76281980&t=50)** So the expected value for scenario 1, which is 30% likely with a $5,000 value, is 30% times 5,000 equals 1500.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-expected-value-24046708?u=76281980&t=61)** You can use the same calculations to calculate expected value for scenario 2 of 4,000, and scenario 3 of -3,000.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-expected-value-24046708?u=76281980&t=70)** You then add up all the expected values to get the overall expected value for a scenario.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-expected-value-24046708?u=76281980&t=76)** So that would be 1,500 plus 4,000 minus 3,000 equals positive $2,500.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-expected-value-24046708?u=76281980&t=84)** So if you're evaluating an investment, this appears to be a good way to spend your money, unless you can find something better to do.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-expected-value-24046708?u=76281980&t=91)** Now switch over to Excel so that we can see how to implement this in a worksheet.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-expected-value-24046708?u=76281980&t=97)** I have switched over to my Excel sample file, and that is 06_03_Expected Value.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-expected-value-24046708?u=76281980&t=102)** You can find it in the chapter six folder of the exercise files collection.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-expected-value-24046708?u=76281980&t=107)** Here I have three scenarios, and I have assigned different probabilities and values to them.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-expected-value-24046708?u=76281980&t=112)** You can see the probabilities in column B and the values and column C.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-expected-value-24046708?u=76281980&t=118)** And as noted in the first part of this video, I want to multiply the probability by the value of a particular scenario to get its expected value.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-expected-value-24046708?u=76281980&t=129)** So I will select cell D2, type an equal sign, and I'll multiply B2, the probability of scenario 1, by the value in C2, which is the value, and I'll press Enter.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-expected-value-24046708?u=76281980&t=146)** And I see the expected value of scenario 1, $50,000 with a 25% probability is $12,500.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-expected-value-24046708?u=76281980&t=156)** Now, I will use the fill handle to drag the contents of cell D2 so that it covers D3 and D4, and I get those calculations there.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-expected-value-24046708?u=76281980&t=168)** So I have 60% times minus 25,000 equals minus 15,000.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-expected-value-24046708?u=76281980&t=173)** And then for scenario 3, 15% times 14,000 is 2,100.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-expected-value-24046708?u=76281980&t=180)** Now to calculate the total expected value for this business proposition, I can click and cell B5, and I'll create an auto sum formula.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-expected-value-24046708?u=76281980&t=190)** So I'll press ALT+=, I get some D2 to D4, and Enter, and I see that I have an expected value of minus $400.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-expected-value-24046708?u=76281980&t=200)** So that means according to my estimates, I am likely to lose money on this investment and I should not go ahead with it.

#### Calculate permutations without duplication
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-permutations-without-duplication-24046711?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript

>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-permutations-without-duplication-24046711?u=76281980&t=8)** When the order matters, you are dealing with what are called permutation.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-permutations-without-duplication-24046711?u=76281980&t=13)** As an example, let's assume that you have eight individuals who have offered to speak at a company event but you can only choose three.

#### Calculate combinations with duplication

> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-combinations-with-duplication-24050671?u=76281980&t=10)** However, we assumed that duplication was not allowed, so you couldn't pick two of the same thing.

> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-combinations-with-duplication-24050671?u=76281980&t=89)** As before, I have the number of available products in cell B3, and the number of items of the sample basket, in other words, the number chosen, in B4.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-combinations-with-duplication-24050671?u=76281980&t=101)** So I'll click in cell B7, type an equal sign.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-combinations-with-duplication-24050671?u=76281980&t=105)** And the function we use for combinations with duplication is COMBINA.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-combinations-with-duplication-24050671?u=76281980&t=112)** And this returns the number of combinations with repetitions allowed for a given number of items.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-combinations-with-duplication-24050671?u=76281980&t=118)** So I'll make sure COMBINA is highlighted. Press tab.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-combinations-with-duplication-24050671?u=76281980&t=122)** And now we need to identify the number, that's the number of items available, so that's in B3.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-combinations-with-duplication-24050671?u=76281980&t=129)** Type a comma, and then I'll click B4, the number chosen, right parenthesis, then enter.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-combinations-with-duplication-24050671?u=76281980&t=136)** And instead of having 10 combinations without duplications, if we allow duplication, we have 35.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-combinations-with-duplication-24050671?u=76281980&t=144)** Now let's see what happens when we increase the number of available products and the number chosen.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-combinations-with-duplication-24050671?u=76281980&t=149)** So I'll start in B3, and I'll change the value from five to eight and press enter.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-combinations-with-duplication-24050671?u=76281980&t=155)** And we go from 10 and 35 to 56 and 120.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-combinations-with-duplication-24050671?u=76281980&t=161)** If I allow four items to be selected instead of just three, out of the eight, we go from 56 and 120, up to 70 and 330.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-combinations-with-duplication-24050671?u=76281980&t=174)** And again, the number of combinations increases more slowly than permutations, because for permutations, order matters, and for combinations it doesn't.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/calculate-combinations-with-duplication-24050671?u=76281980&t=183)** But I'm sure you can see that as we increase the number of available products and the number of items in the sample basket, that combinations both with and without duplication will increase very quickly.

#### Solution: Calculate the expected value of a business scenario
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-calculate-the-expected-value-of-a-business-scenario-24044686?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-calculate-the-expected-value-of-a-business-scenario-24044686?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In the previous movie, I described a set of four scenarios, assigning probabilities and values of that scenario to each one.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-calculate-the-expected-value-of-a-business-scenario-24044686?u=76281980&t=14)** And our goal is to calculate the expected value of each of the scenarios and then also for the entire project.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-calculate-the-expected-value-of-a-business-scenario-24044686?u=76281980&t=22)** So I'll start in cell D2, and the expected value of a particular scenario is the probability multiplied by the value.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-calculate-the-expected-value-of-a-business-scenario-24044686?u=76281980&t=33)** So in D2, I'll type equal and I have B2 times C2 and Enter.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-calculate-the-expected-value-of-a-business-scenario-24044686?u=76281980&t=40)** So we have 18% of 30,000, which is $5,400.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-calculate-the-expected-value-of-a-business-scenario-24044686?u=76281980&t=45)** And now I can copy that formula down so that I have the same calculation for the other scenarios.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-calculate-the-expected-value-of-a-business-scenario-24044686?u=76281980&t=53)** So with cell D2 selected, I will drag the fill handle down, cover the other cells, and I can see that I have 5% or 1/20 of 60,000 of 3000, 1/3 of 5,000 of 1650, and 44% of 10,000 is 4,400.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-calculate-the-expected-value-of-a-business-scenario-24044686?u=76281980&t=73)** Note that the values for scenarios three and four are negative, that's why they're in parentheses.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-calculate-the-expected-value-of-a-business-scenario-24044686?u=76281980&t=79)** To find the total expected value, we want to add up all of the expected values from the individual scenarios.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-calculate-the-expected-value-of-a-business-scenario-24044686?u=76281980&t=86)** So I'll click cell D6, press Alt + Equal which creates an auto sum formula.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-calculate-the-expected-value-of-a-business-scenario-24044686?u=76281980&t=92)** D2 to D5, looks good, Enter.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-calculate-the-expected-value-of-a-business-scenario-24044686?u=76281980&t=95)** And I get an expected value of 2,350.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-calculate-the-expected-value-of-a-business-scenario-24044686?u=76281980&t=101)** And we can also do a crosscheck to make sure our probabilities add up to 100 by also creating an auto sum formula in B6.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-calculate-the-expected-value-of-a-business-scenario-24044686?u=76281980&t=108)** So Alt + Equal and Enter, and they do add up to 100%.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-calculate-the-expected-value-of-a-business-scenario-24044686?u=76281980&t=112)** So everything looks good.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-calculate-the-expected-value-of-a-business-scenario-24044686?u=76281980&t=114)** So we have an expected value of $2,350.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-calculate-the-expected-value-of-a-business-scenario-24044686?u=76281980&t=118)** It is a positive value, but the question is whether we would want to go forward with it given the high probability of failure.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-calculate-the-expected-value-of-a-business-scenario-24044686?u=76281980&t=125)** If we look at the probabilities for scenarios three and four, we see that 77% of the time we are going to lose money.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-calculate-the-expected-value-of-a-business-scenario-24044686?u=76281980&t=134)** However, if we assume that our initial investment is $10,000, then 2,350 represents about a 23.5% return.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-calculate-the-expected-value-of-a-business-scenario-24044686?u=76281980&t=144)** So based on your tolerance for risk, you might want to go ahead with this project.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-calculate-the-expected-value-of-a-business-scenario-24044686?u=76281980&t=150)** However, if you have other projects that might return a lower expected value but have higher probability of success, then you might choose to go in that direction.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-calculate-the-expected-value-of-a-business-scenario-24044686?u=76281980&t=159)** And that's what makes business so challenging.

### 7. Performing Bayesian Analysis

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### Introduce Bayesian analysis
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-bayesian-analysis-24050672?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-bayesian-analysis-24050672?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Previously, in this course, I have discussed what are known as descriptive statistics.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-bayesian-analysis-24050672?u=76281980&t=5)** Descriptive statistics, as the name implies, provide facts about your data.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-bayesian-analysis-24050672?u=76281980&t=10)** Those can include measures such as medians, means, variance, and standard deviation.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-bayesian-analysis-24050672?u=76281980&t=16)** You can then use these facts about your data to make estimates at a known confidence level.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-bayesian-analysis-24050672?u=76281980&t=21)** For example, you might think that most of your customers live within a 25 mile radius, plus or minus three or four miles, and you might be wondering, "What else is there?"
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-bayesian-analysis-24050672?u=76281980&t=32)** It turns out there's a lot more. Let me give you an example.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-bayesian-analysis-24050672?u=76281980&t=37)** Let's assume for the sake of argument that you might have the flu and your test for the flu has come back positive.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-bayesian-analysis-24050672?u=76281980&t=45)** The test has the following characteristics, and all of these will be important for our analysis: It returns the correct result 85% of the time.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-bayesian-analysis-24050672?u=76281980&t=55)** It identifies healthy individuals as having the flu 10% of the time.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-bayesian-analysis-24050672?u=76281980&t=59)** So in other words, if you do have the flu, you will get a positive result 85% of the time, a negative result 15% of the time.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-bayesian-analysis-24050672?u=76281980&t=67)** If you don't have the flu, then you'll get a positive result 10% of the time, a false positive, and you'll get the correct result of negative the other 90%.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-bayesian-analysis-24050672?u=76281980&t=78)** We also have a base rate, which assumes that about 1% of the population actually has the flu at the time you take the test.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-bayesian-analysis-24050672?u=76281980&t=88)** So the question is, what is the probability that you actually have the flu?
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-bayesian-analysis-24050672?u=76281980&t=93)** For this, we use Bayes' rule.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-bayesian-analysis-24050672?u=76281980&t=95)** We combine the accuracy, false positive, and base rate to find the result.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-bayesian-analysis-24050672?u=76281980&t=100)** So if 1% of the population has the flu and the test is 85% accurate, but there's also a 10% false-positive rate, a positive test means the probability that you actually have the flu is: 7.91%.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-bayesian-analysis-24050672?u=76281980&t=120)** How we got to that number is the subject of the rest of this chapter.

>

> **CLI Commands:** make (1), find (1)
> **Definitions:** known as (1), in other words (1)
> **Analogies:** such as (1), for example (1)
