#### Analyze a sample problem: Kahneman’s Cabs
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-sample-problem-kahneman-s-cabs-24050670?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-sample-problem-kahneman-s-cabs-24050670?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] If you haven't worked with Bayesian analysis before dealing with prior probabilities and base rates, then the concept can be a little unclear.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-sample-problem-kahneman-s-cabs-24050670?u=76281980&t=8)** I'd like to work through a classic example from Tversky and Kahneman called it the taxicab problem that will demonstrate how Bayesian analysis works and give you better intuition.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-sample-problem-kahneman-s-cabs-24050670?u=76281980&t=19)** Let's start with the facts as proposed within the problem, A cab was involved in a hit-and-run accident at night.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-sample-problem-kahneman-s-cabs-24050670?u=76281980&t=27)** There are two cab companies working in the city, green and blue.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-sample-problem-kahneman-s-cabs-24050670?u=76281980&t=33)** Furthermore, we know that a witness identified the cab as blue.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-sample-problem-kahneman-s-cabs-24050670?u=76281980&t=39)** However, 85% of the cabs in the city are green and only 15% are blue.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-sample-problem-kahneman-s-cabs-24050670?u=76281980&t=46)** Next, we need to know about witness reliability.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-sample-problem-kahneman-s-cabs-24050670?u=76281980&t=49)** The court tested the reliability of the witness under the circumstances that existed on the night of the accident, and concluded that the witness correctly identified each one of the two colors 80% of the time and failed 20% of the time.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-sample-problem-kahneman-s-cabs-24050670?u=76281980&t=66)** If there are any attorneys watching this video, don't worry, this is merely an example and it's not meant to be taken as a reflection of witness reliability in the real world.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-sample-problem-kahneman-s-cabs-24050670?u=76281980&t=80)** So your question is, what is the probability that the cab involved in the accident was actually blue rather than green?
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-sample-problem-kahneman-s-cabs-24050670?u=76281980&t=89)** So we're looking at the probabilities for blue versus green.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-sample-problem-kahneman-s-cabs-24050670?u=76281980&t=94)** The answer might surprise you.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-sample-problem-kahneman-s-cabs-24050670?u=76281980&t=96)** And Neel Ocean in an internet posting provides an intuitive explanation of how to arrive at the correct answer, which is 41%.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-sample-problem-kahneman-s-cabs-24050670?u=76281980&t=106)** And you can find the answer at the website listed below.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-sample-problem-kahneman-s-cabs-24050670?u=76281980&t=112)** Let's walk through Neel Ocean's explanation of why the answer is 41%.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-sample-problem-kahneman-s-cabs-24050670?u=76281980&t=118)** We need to visualize the answer as a decision tree.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-sample-problem-kahneman-s-cabs-24050670?u=76281980&t=122)** At the top, we know that our base rate of cabs in the city are 85% green and 15% blue.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-sample-problem-kahneman-s-cabs-24050670?u=76281980&t=131)** The witness is 80% accurate.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-sample-problem-kahneman-s-cabs-24050670?u=76281980&t=133)** So if a cab is green, then the witness will say green 80% of the time and be correct and will be wrong 20% of the time, meaning that the cab is actually blue.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-sample-problem-kahneman-s-cabs-24050670?u=76281980&t=144)** And we have the same for the blue side.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-sample-problem-kahneman-s-cabs-24050670?u=76281980&t=146)** If the cab is actually blue, then it will be guessed 80% of the time.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-sample-problem-kahneman-s-cabs-24050670?u=76281980&t=151)** If it's green, then 20% of the time the witness will be wrong.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-sample-problem-kahneman-s-cabs-24050670?u=76281980&t=156)** Now we take compound probabilities to see how likely it is we end up in each branch of this decision tree.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-sample-problem-kahneman-s-cabs-24050670?u=76281980&t=163)** So 85% of the time the cab is green and it is guessed as being green.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-sample-problem-kahneman-s-cabs-24050670?u=76281980&t=169)** 80% of the time, 0.85 times 0.8 is 0.68.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-sample-problem-kahneman-s-cabs-24050670?u=76281980&t=174)** Same thing for if the cab is actually blue but it's guessed green.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-sample-problem-kahneman-s-cabs-24050670?u=76281980&t=180)** We have 0.85 times 0.2, which leads to 0.17.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-sample-problem-kahneman-s-cabs-24050670?u=76281980&t=185)** And then we have similar calculations for blue, leading to probabilities of 0.12 and 0.03.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-sample-problem-kahneman-s-cabs-24050670?u=76281980&t=194)** Now remember that the witness said the cab is blue, so we need to calculate the probability that the witness guessed blue when the cab was actually blue.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-sample-problem-kahneman-s-cabs-24050670?u=76281980&t=206)** So we use the two values in the middle where the cab was actually blue and we get this calculation, 0.12 when the cab is actually blue divided by 0.12 plus 0.17, which is when the cab was blue and was guessed blue, but also when the cab was guess green but was actually blue.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-sample-problem-kahneman-s-cabs-24050670?u=76281980&t=230)** So we have 0.12 divided by 0.12 plus 0.17, and that leads to a result of 0.41.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/analyze-a-sample-problem-kahneman-s-cabs-24050670?u=76281980&t=239)** In the next movie, I will show you how to create what is called a classification matrix so we can get the values that we need to perform this calculation in Excel.

#### Challenge: Perform a Bayesian analysis
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/challenge-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24050669?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/challenge-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24050669?u=76281980&t=0)** (bright music)
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/challenge-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24050669?u=76281980&t=5)** - [Instructor] Throughout this chapter, I have shown you how to perform Bayesian analysis in Excel.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/challenge-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24050669?u=76281980&t=10)** In this movie, I would like to challenge you to solve a problem on your own using the techniques that you learned earlier.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/challenge-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24050669?u=76281980&t=17)** Let's assume that a museum is reviewing objects in a particular collection, and it has determined, unfortunately, that only about 70% of the items in that collection are genuine and the rest are counterfeit.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/challenge-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24050669?u=76281980&t=32)** Also, it believes, based on this experience, that the appraisers and curators will be correcting their identification 90% of the time, whether an object is genuine or counterfeit.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/challenge-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24050669?u=76281980&t=44)** So your goal is to fill in this worksheet and to determine the probability that an item is genuine when reported genuine, and also when it is reported counterfeit and actually counterfeit.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/challenge-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24050669?u=76281980&t=59)** I hope you enjoy this problem, and I will come back in the next movie to show you how I would solve it.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Microsoft Excel|Excel]] (1), [[Representational State Transfer (REST)|Rest]] (1), [[Probability]] (1), next (1)
> **Cross-References:** in the next (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### Solution: Perform a Bayesian analysis
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In the previous movie I described a problem where you will determine the probability an item is genuine when it's reported genuine and an item is counterfeit when reported counterfeit by museum appraisers and curators based on the base rate of genuine items comprising 70% of the collection and an appraiser accuracy rate of 90%.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=27)** So let's go ahead and fill in the worksheet and perform our calculations.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=31)** I'll click on cell B six.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=34)** The probability an item is genuine is given in B three.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=37)** So in B six I'll type equal and B three.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=41)** That means that the probability it is counterfeit or fake, not real is one minus 70%.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=48)** So in B seven I'll type equal one minus and our base rates in B three.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=52)** So enter 70 and 30 is a hundred, which is correct.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=56)** Now we need to determine the probability the appraisers are correct or incorrect.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=62)** So in B nine I'll type equal the probability they're correct is their accuracy.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=68)** So that's in B four.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=70)** And as with the probability of an item being counterfeit, the probability of them being incorrect is one minus the probability they are correct.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=79)** So in B 10 equal one minus B four and enter, and we get 10%, 90 plus 10 equals 100.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=87)** So we're good there.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=89)** Now we can go over to our classification matrix and calculate the probability of each one of our scenarios.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=96)** So we have an item actually being genuine and being reported genuine.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=103)** So in E four I'll type equal and the base rate of them being genuine is 70% and I have that probability in B six.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=114)** And then I want to multiply that by the probability that the curator is correct, that's in B nine and enter.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=122)** So that's the probability for that scenario.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=124)** Now an E five, I need to give the probability that an item is reported as genuine but it's actually counterfeit.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=133)** So in other words, a mistake was made.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=135)** So equal, and I will have an item is being counterfeit.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=143)** That is in B seven.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=145)** And we'll multiply that by the probability that the appraiser is incorrect and that is in cell B 10 and enter.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=155)** So we have 3%.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=157)** Now we have reported counterfeit and actually genuine.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=161)** So again, a mistake was made.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=163)** So in F four we'll type an equal sign and we're multiplying the probability that the item is actually genuine.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=170)** That is in B six by the probability that the appraiser made a mistake.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=177)** That's in B 10 enter, so that's 7%.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=179)** And then in F five we need to calculate the probability that an appraiser correctly identified a counterfeit object.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=186)** So in F five equal and we are multiplying the probability that the item is counterfeit.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=192)** That's in B seven by the probability that the appraiser is correct, that's in B nine enter and we get 27% and we can look at our values to make sure that everything lines up.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=205)** So our correct guesses are 63% and 27% of the time that's 90, that is our probability correct.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=212)** And seven plus three is 10, and that is the probability of the appraiser being incorrect.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=219)** Now we can calculate the probability that an item is genuine when reported genuine and counterfeit when reported counterfeit.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=225)** So click on cell E eight type an equal sign.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=229)** And we need to calculate the percentage of times that an item is reported as genuine and it actually is so reported genuine is in cell E four divided by the total number of times an item is identified as genuine, both right and wrong.
>
> **[4:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=246)** And that is adding E four and E five.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=249)** So type left parentheses E four plus E five and tab and we get 95%.
>
> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=258)** Now we can calculate the probability and the item is actually counterfeit when it's reported counterfeit.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=265)** So in F eight I'll type equal and we need to divide the correct guesses by total guesses.
>
> **[4:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=271)** For this column, the correct guesses are an F five.
>
> **[4:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=274)** Then I'll type a forward slash for division.
>
> **[4:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=278)** And we need to add the total number of times that an item is reported as counterfeit, both right and wrong.
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=284)** So that's F four plus F five, right parentheses and enter.
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=288)** And we get 79%.
>
> **[4:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=290)** And so it appears that our team will do a very good job of identifying genuine items with very little error and still a pretty good, but not quite as good job identifying counterfeits.
>
> **[5:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=302)** I hope you enjoyed this problem.
>
> **[5:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=304)** Bayesian analysis is very different from other types analysis that we typically do in Excel.
>
> **[5:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=309)** So don't worry if you didn't get it right on the first time.
>
> **[5:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-a-bayesian-analysis-24049635?u=76281980&t=313)** Take a few minutes, walk away from the workbook, come back and feel free to try again.

### 8. Performing Regression Analysis

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### Introduce linear regression
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-linear-regression?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] One very useful technique in data analysis is least-squares regression.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=6)** The idea is that you have two or more sets of numerical values and you draw a straight line that best describes the dataset.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=15)** Your goal is to find the line that minimizes the total error across all of your data points, and it begins with a guess, and from there, you repeat the process.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=27)** You start by visualizing your data points.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=30)** Here, I have a collection of sales data.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=33)** The number of customers for a given day appears on the X axis, and the sales for that day appear on the Y axis.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=41)** And each point represents the intersection of the number of customers and sales.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=47)** And again, our goal is to draw a line that best fits the data.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=52)** So what does it mean to find the line of best fit?
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=56)** Well, here's the line that best describes the data, and our goal is to minimize the error between that line and each of the points.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=66)** And we square that value, the distance between a particular dot and the line, so that we never have negative values.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=76)** If we didn't square the values, we could have a day where the prediction was $100 too high and another where the prediction was $100 too low, and those two would cancel out and say that there was zero error.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=89)** By squaring, we always end up with a positive result, and that allows us to perform our analysis.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=96)** The equation for this line is 13.15 multiplied by the number of customers plus a value of 220.78.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=109)** That does give us the somewhat nonsensical result that, if we had zero customers, we would still have $220.78 in sales.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=118)** But again, we are just creating a line that fits the data that we have in our collection.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=125)** So what are some of the limits of this technique?
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=128)** Well, it's unlikely for any prediction to be exactly right, and in fact, if you find that you have a line that exactly fits the data, then the data you're examining is determined by a function and you don't need to do this kind of analysis.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=144)** There are also many factors that you don't or can't measure.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=148)** For instance, the temperature or weather on a given day might discourage customers from coming in, or you might happen to have several customers from out of town who place large orders all at the same time.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=160)** There is no way to predict those elements, so you're not able to incorporate them into the equation directly.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=167)** Also, it's important to remember that regression is only valid for the data range that you have collected.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=173)** So if you want to look below the minimum number of customers or beyond the maximum number of customers in the example that I gave, you will need to collect more data.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=184)** So in total, you use regression for information but not for definition.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/introduce-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=189)** It gives you an effective summary of the data that you've collected and it can provide insights, but should not be taken as truth by itself.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** data (12)
> **CLI Commands:** find (3)
> **Versions:** 220.78 (2), 13.15 (1)
> **Analogies:** for instance (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

>

>

#### Perform linear regression on a single variable

#### Interpret linear regression results
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-linear-regression-results?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-linear-regression-results?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In the previous movie, we used the Analysis Toolpak's regression feature to perform linear regression on a set of variables.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-linear-regression-results?u=76281980&t=9)** We had an independent variable of the number of customers that came into a store and a dependent variable on the sales from those customers.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-linear-regression-results?u=76281980&t=19)** In this movie, I'll show you how to interpret the output.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-linear-regression-results?u=76281980&t=23)** My sample file is 08_04_interpret, and you can find it in the chapter 8 folder of the Exercise Files collection.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-linear-regression-results?u=76281980&t=31)** We put the output for our linear regression onto a new worksheet.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-linear-regression-results?u=76281980&t=36)** The original data is still on Sheet1, which you can see here, and the results of the analysis are on Sheet2.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-linear-regression-results?u=76281980&t=46)** At the top, we have our regression statistics.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-linear-regression-results?u=76281980&t=50)** The first is multiple R, that's in cell B4.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-linear-regression-results?u=76281980&t=55)** Multiple R is the correlation between our two sets of data, so we have the number of customers and the sales.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-linear-regression-results?u=76281980&t=63)** And it turns out that there is a fairly high correlation, basically 0.71, between our two variables.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-linear-regression-results?u=76281980&t=71)** So, it does appear that the number of customers has a significant impact on the sales for the day, and that makes sense.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-linear-regression-results?u=76281980&t=82)** The next question is how well the analysis and the equation that we've created describes our data.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-linear-regression-results?u=76281980&t=92)** Well, here, we have the R squared value in B5, and this is a goodness of fit measure that indicates how well the line that we would draw to predict the values, and that is the output of linear regression describes the data.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-linear-regression-results?u=76281980&t=111)** We have an R square of 50% and that is actually not that great.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-linear-regression-results?u=76281980&t=118)** In other words, we're seeing a lot of variation beyond what is described by the number of customers.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-linear-regression-results?u=76281980&t=127)** So, the way I would interpret this result is that the number of customers tells you a little bit about how much you're going to have in sales for the day, but there are many other factors, perhaps ones that we can't predict.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-linear-regression-results?u=76281980&t=143)** We don't need to worry about adjusted R square.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-linear-regression-results?u=76281980&t=145)** That is used if you have multiple values and you're performing multiple regression, and then the number of observations is 31, and that matches our original dataset.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-linear-regression-results?u=76281980&t=157)** The next question is whether the number of customers is a significant indicator of sales for the day.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-linear-regression-results?u=76281980&t=165)** And for that, we need to go down to the bottom, look for customers, and this is why I included labels when we performed the linear regression in the previous movie.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-linear-regression-results?u=76281980&t=177)** And from there, we look to the right for the P-value for the customer's row.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-linear-regression-results?u=76281980&t=184)** In general, and this measure or rule of thumb causes a great deal of discussion among statisticians, but the general rule is that any P-value below 0.05 indicates that relationship between two variables is significant.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-linear-regression-results?u=76281980&t=206)** In other words, there's a 1 out of 20 chance that the measurement that we saw is due to luck and not because of some causality.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-linear-regression-results?u=76281980&t=218)** In this case, we have a P-value for the number of customers of 8.49 times E to the minus 6, which indicates six zeros before the 8, so we have a decimal point, six zeros, and then the number 8.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-linear-regression-results?u=76281980&t=233)** So, it is very unlikely that customers have a rephrase from.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-linear-regression-results?u=76281980&t=239)** So, it's very unlikely.
>
> **[4:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-linear-regression-results?u=76281980&t=242)** So, it is very unlikely that the number of customers has nothing to do with the sales for the day, and that makes sense.
>
> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-linear-regression-results?u=76281980&t=251)** The more people you have come in, the more opportunities that they have to rephrase from, and that makes sense.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-linear-regression-results?u=76281980&t=259)** And that makes sense.
>
> **[4:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-linear-regression-results?u=76281980&t=260)** The more people that come into your store, the more chances you have to make a sale.
>
> **[4:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-linear-regression-results?u=76281980&t=267)** There's a lot more going on, but for this introduction to linear regression, I think we've done enough.
>
> **[4:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/interpret-linear-regression-results?u=76281980&t=273)** Just remember that you are looking at the correlation, which is multiple R, the R squared, which is how well the model describes your data, and also the P-value for the independent variable, which is a measure of how well the independent variable describes the value of the related dependent variable.

>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/perform-multiple-regression?u=76281980&t=221)** And we have a correlation of .75, so there is a strong relationship.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/perform-multiple-regression?u=76281980&t=228)** Because we are performing multiple regression, we skip over the R square and go to adjusted R square, and here we have a goodness of fit of about .53, so a little more than half of sales is described by the customer count and the number of postcards.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/perform-multiple-regression?u=76281980&t=252)** Now let's go down to our results down below and look at the effect of customers and postcards separately.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/perform-multiple-regression?u=76281980&t=263)** I'll start with customers, and here we look at the probability that customers are significant by examining the P-value.
>
> **[4:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/perform-multiple-regression?u=76281980&t=272)** And here the P value is 3.03 times E to the minus 6, which is a very small result.
>
> **[4:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/perform-multiple-regression?u=76281980&t=279)** As I mentioned in the previous movie, the threshold for significance is usually taken as 0.05, and a value of six zeros to the right of the decimal point before you get to the first three is much lower than .05, so we can see the customers are almost certainly significant.
>
> **[5:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/perform-multiple-regression?u=76281980&t=300)** The same is not true for postcards.
>
> **[5:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/perform-multiple-regression?u=76281980&t=303)** Here we have a P-value of about .37, which is much higher than .05, so it appears that the number of postcards mailed out had no impact on sales for a given day.
>
> **[5:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/perform-multiple-regression?u=76281980&t=319)** If we wanted to run the analysis again, what we might do is just a one-variable analysis.
>
> **[5:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/perform-multiple-regression?u=76281980&t=325)** So going back to sheet one, where we would look only at customers as related to sales and leave postcards out of the analysis because they don't appear to have a significant impact on sales for a given day.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** data (6), [[Linear Regression]] (2), next (2), [[Probability]] (1)
> **UI Navigation:** go to (2), scroll down (2), scroll up (1)

>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/challenge-perform-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=5)** In this movie, I would like to challenge you to perform linear regression on a new data set.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/challenge-perform-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=12)** To demonstrate, I'm using the chapter eight challenge workbook you can find in the chapter eight folder of the exercise files collection.
>

>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/challenge-perform-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=35)** So the independent variable customers is your X data or X input, and sales is the dependent variable or Y input.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/challenge-perform-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=48)** If you haven't already, please install the analysis tool pack add-in, and then use the built-in regression tool to analyze this data.

#### Solution: Perform linear regression
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-linear-regression?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] If you'd like to follow along, you can find the file, Chapter Eight Challenge, in the chapter eight folder of the Exercise Files collection.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=10)** I have my customer in sales data and I need to make sure that the analysis tool pack add in is installed.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=18)** To do that, I'll go to the file tab of the ribbon, click options.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=25)** Then I'll click the add-ins category.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=29)** Bo all the way down to the bottom, and next to Manage, I should see Excel add-ins.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=35)** If I didn't, then I could click the down arrow and select it from the list, then click go.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=43)** Then in the adds in dialogue box, I need to make sure that analysis tool pack is checked.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=48)** It is for me, so I'll click okay.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=51)** I could also click cancel, but if you needed to install it, make sure that box is checked and then click okay.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=60)** I can now find the data analysis tool going to the data tab on the ribbon, and then in the analysis group, click data analysis.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=71)** And the data analysis dialogue box, I'll scroll down, click regression, and then click okay.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=82)** Here I have the regression dialogue box.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=86)** My data has labels, so the first thing I will do is check the labels checkbox, and now I can identify the Y range, which is the dependent variable, and those values are in column B.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=100)** So I'll click the collapse dialogue button next to input Y range, and then I will select sales B-1 through B-32.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=111)** So I have 31 days of values plus the label, and I'll click to expand the dialogue box again.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=119)** Now I need to identify the independent variable that is the X range.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=123)** So I'll click the collapse dialogue control next to that, scroll up, and select cells A-1 through A-32, again, including the label in cell A-1.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=137)** Then I'll expand the regression dialogue box back, and I don't need to make any other changes.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=143)** I will put my results on a new worksheet.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=148)** Everything looks good, so I'll click okay, and I get a new worksheet, called Sheet-2 that has my output, and I will select columns A through I, and double click the edge of one of the column headers.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=164)** That auto fits the columns to the width of the data that appears in the worksheet.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=169)** And I'll click away so that the selection is released.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=175)** So that, in this case, is the number of customers and sales for the day.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=179)** And I see a multiple R value of 0.74, which indicates that there is a fairly strong correlation between the number of customers and the sales for a given day, and that makes sense.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=193)** The more customers that come in, the more chances you have to make a sale.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=198)** In cell B-5, we have R squared, which is how well the line fits the data.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=205)** So in this case, the number of customers explains about 55% of the sales.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=211)** So the number of customers has a significant impact, but there are other things that affect sales as well, and some of those could just be random.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=221)** It could be weather in the area, or it could be a number of customers who come in and make unusually large purchases, and there's absolutely no way to predict that.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=233)** The number of observations is 31, and that fits what we were doing with our data.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=239)** We have one month of data, and it was a 31 day month.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=243)** And finally, we can check how significant the number of customers is in terms of P value.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=252)** So I'll go down to row 18 where customers is and select the P value cell, which is E-18.
>
> **[4:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=261)** The P value for customers is 1.8 times E to the minus six, and in scientific notation, that means we have zero, then the decimal point, followed by six zeroes, followed by 1.8, and the rest of the numbers you see there.
>
> **[4:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=279)** In general, the P value is considered to be significant if it is 0.05 or below.
>
> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=287)** In this case, we have 0.6 zeros followed by the number one, so the P value is significantly, substantially, greatly below 0.05, so it is very likely that the number of customers is a significant indicator of sales on a given day.
>
> **[5:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=307)** I hope you were able to perform this analysis.
>
> **[5:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/solution-perform-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=309)** If you didn't quite get it right the first time, you can always go back, close and reopen the sample file, and do your analysis again.

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/additional-resources-24043676?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Curt] Thanks again for your interest in this course.
>
> **[0:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/additional-resources-24043676?u=76281980&t=2)** Before I go, I'd like to point out some further resources that you can use to learn more about data analysis.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/additional-resources-24043676?u=76281980&t=10)** I have three books in mind for you.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/additional-resources-24043676?u=76281980&t=12)** The first is "Statistics for People Who (Think They) Hate Statistics," by Neil Salkind, who is a professor at the University of Kansas.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/additional-resources-24043676?u=76281980&t=21)** It's a terrific book, and even though it's a little bit older, it's worth looking for.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/additional-resources-24043676?u=76281980&t=26)** Next is, "Head First Data Analysis" by Michael Milton, published by O'Reilly.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/additional-resources-24043676?u=76281980&t=31)** It's a terrific book that presents material visually, so it's a little bit different approach from Neil's book.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/additional-resources-24043676?u=76281980&t=38)** And finally, "Data Analysis and Business Modeling" by Wayne Winston, published by Microsoft Press.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/additional-resources-24043676?u=76281980&t=45)** This book is available in several different editions, so if you're looking to save money, a used copy will do you just fine.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-excel-data-analysis-18868618/additional-resources-24043676?u=76281980&t=53)** Thanks again for your interest in this course, and I hope you have fun analyzing data in Excel.
